Critique of Defining Necessary Existence as Independence from a Cause
Razi critiques the third interpretation and presents an objection against a common argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining the Necessary Being as independent of a cause is insufficient, and why a third rational division—an uncaused but possible existent—poses a challenge to common arguments for God's existence.

Core Idea
Independence from a cause does not guarantee necessity; an uncaused possible being is conceivable.

This passage from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi critically examines a common definition of the Necessary Being as that which is independent of a separate cause. He introduces a rigorous rational division that exposes a subtle flaw: an entity could be uncaused yet still be possible in itself. Razi then presents a common counterargument and an objection that challenges its validity. Understanding this critique is essential for grasping the precision required in arguments for God's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three rational divisions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the three categories of existents: by itself, through another, and neither by itself nor through another.
The third division is the key focus of the critique.
2
Understand the third division and its implications
Focus on the description of the third division: an existent that comes into being from no cause at all, neither from itself nor from another, and is capable of non-existence. Ask yourself why this is problematic for the definition of necessary existence.
Even if this division is false in reality, it is a conceptual possibility.
3
Follow Razi's argument about independence and necessity
Razi argues that as long as the third division is not proven false, we cannot conclude that an entity independent of a cause is necessary. It could be uncaused yet still possible.
This undermines the simple equation: independence = necessity.
4
Examine the common argument dichotomizing existents
Identify the argument that some people make: either an existent is independent of a cause (hence necessary) or dependent (hence contingent). This dichotomy ignores the third division.
This argument is presented as insufficient.
5
Analyze the objector's response
Read the objection: one could conceive of an existent that occurs without any cause (third division), which would be independent yet not necessary. Therefore, the common argument fails unless the third division is refuted.
The objection ends the passage; Razi does not provide an immediate answer here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولنا : إن واجب الوجود لذاته، هو الذي لا يكون وجوده معللا بسبب منفصل ، فهذا أيضا فيه نظر . لأن أقسام الموجودات ، بحسب التقسيم العقلي ، ثلاثة : الذي يكون موجودا لذاته، والذي يكون موجودا لغيره ، والذي يكون موجودا ، لا لذاته ولا لغيره . وهذا القسم الثالث . معناه : أنه تحصيل الوجود للشيء من غير سبب أصلا ، لا من ذاته ، ولا من غيره ويصح أيضا طريان العدم عليه ، من غير سبب أصلا . لا من ذاته ، ولا من غيره ] (۱) وهذا القسم ، وإن كان باطلا ، إلا أنه قسم من الأقسام المعتبرة ، بحسب التقسيم العقلي الأولي. إذا ثبت هذا ، فما لم يظهر فساد هذا القسم ، إما بحسب بديهة العقل أو بحسب الدليل المنفصل ، لم يلزم من كون الشيء غنيا في وجوده عن السبب المنفصل ، كونه واجب الوجود لذاته، لأن على تقدير كون هذا القسم صحيحا ، يكون] (٣) الشيء غنيا في وجوده عن السبب المنفصل ، مع أنه يكون في نفسه قابلا للعدم وللوجود . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : من الناس من لم يعرف هذه التفاصيل ، وقال] (۳) إما أن يكون الموجود غنيا في وجوده عن السبب ، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك ، فإن كان غنيا في وجوده عن السبب [المنفصل] (4) كان واجب الوجود الذاته، وهو المطلوب . وإن لم يكن غنيا في وجوده عن السبب ] (٥) كان متحاجا إلى السبب ، فوجوده يدل على وجود السبب . فيثبت أن الاعتراف بوجود موجود [ غني عن السبب ] (٦) يوجب الاعتراف بوجود موجود واجب الوجود لذاته. ولقائل أن يقول : إن هذا القدر لا يفيد المقصود ، لأنه يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه وجد ، لا لذاته ولا لغيره ، بل حصل لا لأمر أصلا مع أن ذاته وماهيته قابلة للعدم فهو لأجل أنه حصل لا لسبب أصلا ، كان غنيا عن السبب ، ولأجل أن حقيقته قابلة للعدم ، لم يكن واجب الوجود لذاته ، فما لم تبطلوا هذا القسم ، لا يحصل مطلوبكم
As for our statement: The necessary of existence in itself is the one whose existence is not caused by a separate cause, this too requires consideration. Because the divisions of existents, according to rational division, are three: that which exists in itself, that which exists through another, and that which exists neither in itself nor through another. This third division means: the attainment of existence for a thing from no cause at all, neither from itself nor from another. And it is also possible for non-existence to occur to it from no cause at all, neither from itself nor from another (1). This division, even if it is false, is still a division among the considered divisions according to the primary rational division. If this is established, then as long as the falsity of this division is not shown, either by mental intuition or by a separate proof, it does not follow from a thing being independent in its existence from the separate cause that it is necessary of existence in itself. Because, assuming this division is correct, the thing would be independent in its existence from the separate cause while being in itself capable of non-existence and existence. If you know this, then we say: Some people did not know these details, and said: Either the existent is independent in its existence from the cause, or it is not. If it is independent in its existence from the [separate] cause, then it is necessary of existence in itself, and that is what is sought. And if it is not independent in its existence from the cause, then it is in need of a cause, so its existence indicates the existence of the cause. Thus it is established that the acknowledgment of the existence of an existent [independent of the cause] necessitates the acknowledgment of the existence of an existent that is necessary of existence in itself. And an objector may say: This amount does not achieve the objective, because it is said: Why is it not permissible to say: It came into existence neither from itself nor from another, but rather it occurred for no reason at all, while its essence and quiddity are capable of non-existence. So because it occurred from no cause at all, it is independent of the cause, and because its reality is capable of non-existence, it is not necessary of existence in itself. Thus, unless you invalidate this division, your goal is not achieved.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage critiques the third interpretation and then presents an objection. The author first notes that defining necessary existence as 'independent of a separate cause' is problematic because existents can be divided rationally into three categories: (1) that which exists by itself (necessary), (2) that which exists through another (contingent), and (3) that which exists neither by itself nor through another—meaning existence comes from no cause at all. This third category, though false in reality, is a conceptual possibility in a primary rational division. As long as its falsity is not proven, a thing being independent of a cause does not imply it is necessary; it could be merely uncaused yet still possible in itself (capable of existence and non-existence). The author then describes a common argument by some who ignore this nuance: they dichotomize existents into independent of cause (which they deem necessary) and dependent on cause. They conclude that admitting an independent existent proves a necessary being. An objector responds by saying that this argument fails because one could conceive of an existent that occurs without any cause, which would be independent of cause yet still possible in itself (capable of non-existence). Therefore, unless the objector's hypothetical third division is refuted, the conclusion that an independent existent is necessary does not follow. The objection ends here without the author's immediate answer, leaving the argument open.

Key Terms
تقسيم عقلي
موجود لذاته
موجود لغيره
موجود لا لذاته ولا لغيره
غني عن السبب
قابل للعدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Independence from a cause is sufficient for necessity.
Correction: An entity could be uncaused yet still be possible in itself (capable of existence and non-existence), so independence does not guarantee necessity.

Checkpoint
What is the third division of existents according to rational division?
A) That which exists by itself
B) That which exists through another
C) That which exists neither by itself nor through another
D) That which does not exist

Key Takeaway
The definition of necessary existence as independent of a cause is inadequate unless the possibility of an uncaused but possible being is refuted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the existence of the universe, do you automatically assume that everything must have a cause? How might this lesson challenge you to reconsider the possibility of uncaused things that are still contingent?
?
Razi's critique forces you to examine the categories you use in reasoning about God. Are there any other assumptions you hold about God's attributes that might be based on incomplete dichotomies? How can you guard against such oversimplifications?
?
If the third division were not refuted, what would that mean for your belief that God is necessary? How does this lesson deepen your conviction that the Necessary Being must be defined in a way that precludes any uncaused possible being?

Is Necessity of Existence a Positive Attribute? The First Proof
The author poses the question and presents the first proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why essential necessity (al-wujūb al-dhātī) is considered a positive, real attribute based on the first proof presented by the author.

Core Idea
Essential necessity is a real attribute, not just a mental construct.

This lesson tackles a foundational question in Islamic theology: Is the necessity of existence a positive attribute or merely a concept? The author begins by defining essential necessity and then offers the first proof that it is a positive matter. Though the passage is incomplete, the core argument is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the question posed at the beginning.
The passage is incomplete but the main argument is present.
2
Identify the definition
Find the definition of essential necessity (al-wujūb al-dhātī). What does it mean for an essence to necessitate its own existence?
3
Analyze the argument
The author argues that deservingness of existence is not merely mental. Why is that important for the debate?
If it were mental, it would not be a real attribute.
4
Examine the analogy
The author uses an analogy about a body and space. How does this analogy support the claim that necessity is positive?
The relation of body to space is real; similarly, necessity is real.
5
Reflect on the source of confusion
The author explains that confusion arises from failing to distinguish between concepts. How does this insight help clarify the issue?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية في أن وجوب الوجود هل هو من قوم برقي أم لا؟ من الناس من قال : هذا المفهوم : مفهوم وجودي محصل . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إنا بينا أن المراد من الوجوب الذاتي كون تلك الماهية مقتضية لوجود نفسها وكونها مستحقة للوجود من ذاتها، وهذا الاستحقاق ليس حصوله بسبب فرض العقل واعتبار الذهن فقط لأن هذا المعنى حاصل سواء اعتبره العقل أو لم يعتبره ، ولو جاز أن لا يكون اقتضاء الوجود وصفا وجوديا مع أنه في نفسه نسبة متحققة محصلة ، لجاز أن يقال : إن نسبة الجسم إلى الحيز بالحصول في نفسه ، ليس أمرا ثبوتيا ، بل أمرا عدميا . وعند هذا يظهر للمنصف : أن الاشتباه الواقع في أن الوجوب هل هو أمر ثبوتي أم لا ؟ كان بسبب عدم التمييز بين المفهومات التي بحثناها وميزنا بين كل واحد منهما وبين الآخر. فتارة يسبق الذهن من الوجود إلى العدم توقفه على الغير ، فيحكم بكونه عدميا ، وتارة إلى كونه عبارة عن استحقاق الوجود ، فيحكم
The second question: Whether the necessity of existence (wujūb al-wujūd) is from [؟] or not? Some people said: This concept is a positive existential concept. And there are proofs for it: The first: We have shown that what is meant by essential necessity (al-wujūb al-dhātī) is that that essence necessitates its own existence and that it deserves existence from itself. And this deservingness is not due to the assumption of the intellect and the consideration of the mind only, because this meaning holds whether the intellect considers it or does not consider it. And if it were permissible that the necessitation of existence were not a positive attribute while it is in itself a real and actual relation, it would be permissible to say: The relation of the body to space by being located in itself is not a positive matter but a privative matter. And here it becomes clear to the fair-minded person that the confusion that occurred regarding whether necessity is a positive matter or not was due to the lack of distinction between the concepts that we have examined and distinguished between each one and the other. Sometimes the mind jumps from existence to non-existence due to its dependence on another, so it judges it to be privative, and sometimes it jumps to its being an expression of deserving existence, so it judges...
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage introduces the second major question of the work: whether wujūb al-wujūd (the necessity of existence) is a positive attribute (amr thubūtī) or not. The first proof is given. The author defines essential necessity (al-wujūb al-dhātī) as the essence's necessitation of its own existence and its deservingness of existence from itself. This deservingness is not merely a mental construct; it holds regardless of whether the intellect considers it. The author then uses an analogy: if the necessitation of existence were not a positive attribute despite being a real relation, then the relation of a body to space (being located in itself) could also be considered privative, which is absurd. Thus, the confusion arises from failing to distinguish different concepts—sometimes the mind mistakenly judges necessity as privative because it depends on another, sometimes as positive because it is the deservingness of existence. The passage cuts off before completing the argument, but it clearly argues for the reality of necessity as a positive attribute.

Key Terms
وجوب الوجود (necessity of existence)
وجوب ذاتي (essential necessity)
مفهوم وجودي محصل (positive existential concept)
نسبة متحققة (real relation)
أمر ثبوتي (positive matter)
أمر عدمي (privative matter)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the necessity of existence is merely a mental consideration and not a real attribute.
Correction: The author argues that the deservingness of existence is real, holding whether the intellect considers it or not, and uses an analogy to show that denying its positivity leads to absurdity.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof given that wujūb al-wujūd is a positive attribute?
A) It is a necessary conclusion of rational division
B) The essence's deservingness of existence is real, not just mental
C) The intellect cannot conceive otherwise
D) All scholars agree on it

Key Takeaway
Essential necessity is a real positive attribute, not merely a mental construct, as demonstrated by the first proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author states that deservingness of existence holds whether you think about it or not. How often does your certainty in Allah's existence depend on your own mental affirmation rather than the unshakable reality of His necessity? What would change in your daily life if you lived with the constant awareness that His necessity is real and independent of your thoughts?
?
Confusion arises from failing to distinguish between concepts. Think of a time you mixed up related ideas about Allah's attributes. What specific confusion might you be carrying right now? Will you resolve to clarify it through study, so your faith is built on distinct and accurate knowledge?
?
If necessity were merely a mental construct, then your entire relationship with Allah could be reduced to an illusion. How does the reality of His necessity challenge you to worship Him with greater sincerity, knowing that He truly deserves all worship from Himself, not because of your acknowledgment?

Allah's Existence

The Necessity of Existence Is Not an Added Attribute
Proof that wujub al-wujud cannot be an added attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the necessity of existence (wujub al-wujud) cannot be an attribute added to the essence of the Necessary Being, and how any attempt to treat it as added leads to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
Necessity cannot be added to essence; it is the essence itself.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a concise proof that the attribute of necessity is not something extra added to the essence of God. The argument proceeds by elimination: if necessity were added, it would either be necessary or possible itself—but both options lead to absurdity. This lesson unpacks each step and shows why necessity must be identical with the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Let the logical flow settle before interpreting.
The passage begins mid-sentence; it assumes the context of discussing necessity as a possible added attribute.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The argument starts by dividing the assumption into two: either the added necessity is itself necessary (بسبب الوجوب) or possible (بسبب الإمكان). Notice how each possibility is treated.
This is a classic division (taqsim) used in kalam proofs.
3
Understand the rejection of the second possibility
The second option (that the added necessity is possible) is ruled out because it would make the Necessary Being possible in essence, which is contradictory. Ask yourself why.
If necessity itself were possible, then the being that has it would be possible, which contradicts the definition of the Necessary Being.
4
Trace the regress from the first possibility
The first option (necessity is necessary) avoids the immediate contradiction, but it leads to an infinite regress: the added necessity would itself need a further necessity, ad infinitum. This too is impossible.
This regress (tasalsul) is a standard argument against added attributes.
5
Conclude that necessity is not added
Since both possibilities fail, the original assumption that necessity is an added attribute must be false. Necessity must be identical with the essence itself.
This conclusion is a cornerstone of tawhid in kalam: God’s attributes are not separate from His essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون على سبيل الوجوب أو على سبيل الإمكان، والثاني باطل، وإلا لكان الوجوب بالذات ممكن الوجوب، فيلزم أن يكون الواجب لذاته ممكنا لذاته وذلك محال. فبقي الأول فنقول: فعل هذا التقدير يكون وجوب الوجوب زائدا عليه، ثم الكلام في الثاني كما في الأول وذلك يوجب التسلسل وهو محال.
It is either by way of necessity or by way of possibility, and the second is false, otherwise the necessary being by itself would be possible of necessity, so it would follow that the necessary being by essence would be possible by essence, and that is impossible. So the first remains, so we say: on this assumption, the necessity of necessity would be added to it, then the discussion of the second is like that of the first, and that necessitates infinite regress, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage provides a proof that the necessity of existence (wujub al-wujud) cannot be an attribute added to the essence of the Necessary Being. The argument begins by considering two possibilities: either the necessity is essential (by way of obligation/necessity) or it is contingent (by way of possibility). The latter is false because it would entail that the Necessary Being is possible by itself, which is contradictory. Therefore, the necessity must be essential. However, if we assume that this essential necessity is an attribute over and above the essence, then the necessity itself would require another necessity to be necessary, leading to an infinite regress. Thus, the necessity cannot be a separate attribute.

Key Terms
الوجوب
الإمكان
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessity is an attribute added to God's essence, like knowledge or power.
Correction: The proof shows that if necessity were added, it would require another necessity, leading to regress. Hence necessity is not a separate attribute but identical with the essence.

Checkpoint
What does this proof conclude about the necessity of existence (wujub al-wujud)?
A) It is an attribute added to the essence.
B) It is the essence itself and cannot be an added attribute.
C) It is a contingent attribute.
D) It leads to a regress of attributes.

Key Takeaway
The necessity of existence is not a separate attribute; it is the essence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak of Allah's existence, do you ever unconsciously treat it as something added to His essence, like a quality He possesses? How does this proof challenge you to purify your understanding of His oneness?
?
This proof shows that even the possibility of regress is impossible for Allah. In what areas of your life do you still rely on infinite chains (e.g., 'I'll repent later,' 'one more sin won't matter') that betray a lack of certainty in His absolute necessity? What step will you take today to cut that chain?
?
If the necessity of existence is not an added attribute but identical to His essence, then every thought you have about Allah must begin from this absolute simplicity. How will this reshape your worship and reliance on Him this week?

Second Proof: Necessity Cannot Be a Separate Entity
Second proof: necessity cannot be entire essence, part, or external attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the necessity of existence cannot be a separate entity, whether as the entire essence, a part, or an external attribute.

Core Idea
Necessity cannot be separate from the essence; all three options lead to contradictions.

This passage presents a powerful argument that the necessity (wujub) of the Necessary Being is not an additional attribute or component. The author systematically eliminates three possible ways necessity could be a separate something—as the whole essence, a part, or an external attribute. Each is shown to lead to absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the trilemma
Grasp the three possibilities being considered: necessity as the entire essence, as a part of the essence, or as something external. Notice that the author declares all three false.
This trilemma is the backbone of the entire argument.
2
Examine why necessity cannot be the entire essence
Read the three sub-arguments: (1) informativeness of predication, (2) known vs unknown essence, (3) necessity as a modal relation. Each shows a difference between the essence and necessity.
These three reasons are distinct but converge on the same conclusion.
3
Examine why necessity cannot be a part
Understand the reasoning: if necessity were a part, the essence would be composite, and every composite is contingent. But the Necessary Being cannot be contingent, so necessity cannot be a part.
This argument relies on the principle that composition entails contingency.
4
Examine why necessity cannot be external
Follow the regress argument: if necessity is external, it would be an attribute dependent on the essence, which is contingent. To be necessary, it would need another necessity, leading to infinite regress and multiple necessities—both impossible.
This is a classic kalam proof against external attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: لو كان الوجوب أمرا ثابتا، لكان إما أن يكون تمام ماهية الواجب، أو جزءا من تلك الماهية، أو أمرا خارجا عنها والكل باطل. أما أنه يمتنع أن يكون تمام الماهية فلوجوه: الأول: إنا إذا قلنا: الجسم واجب الوجود لذاته، كان الكلام مفيدا ولو قلنا: واجب الوجوب واجب الوجوب لم يكن الكلام مفيدا فظهر الفرق. الثاني: إن ماهية واجب الوجود لذاته غير معلومة وكونه واجب الوجود لذاته معلوم، فوجب التغاير. الثالث: إن وجوب الوجود كيفية لانتساب الموضوع إلى المحمول. ولذلك قال أهل المنطق: الجهات الثلاثة: الوجوب والإمكان والامتناع. وإذا كذلك امتنع أن يكون الوجوب تمام الماهية. أما أنه يمتنع أن يكون الوجوب جزءا من الماهية. فلأن كل ما كان كذلك كان مركبا، وكل مركب فهو ممكن لذاته، وكل ممكن لذاته فإنما يجب بإيجاب غيره، فيلزم أن يكون الوجوب الذاتي ممكنا لذاته، واجبا بغيره، وهو محال. وأما أنه يمتنع أن يكون الوجوب خارجا عن الماهية، فلان بتقدير أن يكون الأمر كذلك، لم يكن جوهرا قائما بنفسه، مباينا عن الذات الواجبة، بل يكون صفة لتلك الذات، والصفة مفتقرة إلى الموصوف. والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته، واجب بغيره فيجب أن يحصل قبل هذا الوجوب وجوب آخر، حتى يكون ذلك الوجوب السابق علة لوجوب هذا الوجوب اللاحق، فيلزم وقوع التسلسل، ويلزم أن يحصل للذات الواحدة أعدادا من الوجوب. وكل ذلك محال.
Second: If necessity were a fixed thing, then it would either be the entire essence of the necessary, or a part of that essence, or something external to it, and all are false. As for its being impossible to be the entire essence, it is for reasons: First: When we say 'the body is necessary of existence by itself,' the statement is informative, but if we say 'necessary of necessity, necessary of necessity,' the statement would not be informative, so the difference appears. Second: The essence of the necessary of existence by itself is unknown, while its being necessary of existence by itself is known, so difference is necessary. Third: The necessity of existence is a modality of the relation of subject to predicate, and therefore the logicians said: the three modalities are necessity, possibility, and impossibility. And if so, it is impossible for necessity to be the entire essence. As for its being impossible for necessity to be a part of the essence, it is because everything that is like that is composite, and every composite is possible by itself, and everything possible by itself is only necessitated by the necessitation of another, so it would follow that the essential necessity is possible by itself and necessary by another, which is impossible. As for its being impossible for necessity to be external to the essence, it is because on the assumption that it is so, it would not be a substance subsistent by itself, separate from the necessary essence, but rather it would be an attribute of that essence, and the attribute is dependent on the described. And whatever is dependent on another is possible by itself and necessary by another, so there must occur before this necessity another necessity, so that that prior necessity would be the cause of the existence of this subsequent necessity, thus necessitating infinite regress, and necessitating that one essence should have multiple necessities. All that is impossible.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof that necessity (wujub) cannot be a separate entity. It examines three possibilities: (1) necessity is the entire essence of the Necessary Being, (2) necessity is a part of the essence, or (3) necessity is external to the essence. The author argues that all three are impossible. For possibility (1), three reasons are given: first, saying 'body is necessary by itself' is informative, while repeating 'necessary of necessity' is not, showing a difference; second, the essence is unknown but its necessity is known, indicating diversity; third, necessity is a modal relation, so it cannot be the entire essence. For possibility (2), if necessity were a part, the essence would be composite, and every composite is contingent, which contradicts necessary being. For possibility (3), if necessity were external, it would be an attribute dependent on the essence, which is contingent, and to be necessary it would require another necessity, leading to infinite regress. Thus all options are invalid, so necessity cannot be a separate entity.

Key Terms
تمام الماهية
جزء الماهية
خارج الماهية
واجب الوجود
ممكن لذاته
مركب
الوجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think necessity could be an attribute added to the essence, similar to how other attributes are added to contingent beings.
Correction: This passage shows that if necessity were an external attribute, it would be dependent and require another necessity, leading to infinite regress. Thus necessity must be identical with the essence.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the second proof?
A) Necessity is the entire essence of the Necessary Being.
B) Necessity cannot be a separate entity because all three possibilities (entire essence, part, external) lead to contradictions.
C) Necessity is an external attribute that causes the essence.
D) Necessity is a composite of parts.

Key Takeaway
Necessity is not a separate entity; it is identical with the essence of the Necessary Being.

?

?

?
If necessity were a part, composition would imply contingency. Do you ever try to 'add' to your concept of God from your own imagination? How does this argument warn you against anthropomorphism or innovation in your faith?

Third Proof: Wujub al-Dhat Cannot Be an Attribute
Third proof that wujub al-dhat cannot be an attribute, with an objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that necessity by itself cannot be a real attribute, based on its composition and the objection regarding a negative condition.

Core Idea
Necessity by itself cannot be a real attribute because it would be composite and thus contingent.

This passage presents the third argument that wujub al-dhat (necessity by itself) cannot be an existent attribute. The proof shows that wujub al-dhat is composite because it shares a common concept with wujub bil-ghayr but differs by a condition. Composition implies contingency, which contradicts necessary existence. An objection is raised: could it be distinguished by a negative condition? The response begins to analyze the common factor, but the argument is incomplete here.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage ends abruptly; the response is not fully given.
2
Identify the composite nature argument
The author argues that wujub al-dhat is a composite essence because it shares the concept of 'necessity' with wujub bil-ghayr but differs by the condition 'by itself'. This composition implies contingency (imkan).
Contingency is impossible for the Necessary Being.
3
Examine the objection
The objector suggests that the distinction could be a negative condition—the absence of the additional condition found in wujub bil-ghayr. Consider why this might seem plausible.
The author does not accept this objection.
4
Follow the response's direction
The response begins by analyzing the common factor (al-wujub al-dhati). It is either necessary or possible by itself. Each branch leads to a contradiction, but the passage cuts off before completing the argument.
The argument is left incomplete, but the direction is clear: the common factor cannot be part of a composite without contradiction.

الوجه الثالث: في بيان أن الوجوب بالذات يمتنع أن يكون وصفا موجودا: وذلك لأن الوجوب بالذات ماهية مركبة. فهي ممكنة لذاتها. ينتج أن الوجوب لذاته ممكن لذاته، وهو محال. إنما قلنا: إن الوجوب بالذات ماهية مركبة فلان الوجوب بالذات والوجوب بالغير متشاركان في المفهوم من كونه وجوبيا، ويمتاز عن الآخر بكونه وجوبيا بالذات، وكون الآخر وجوبيا بالغير، وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة، فيلزم أن يكون الوجوب بالذات مركبا من هذين القيدين. فإن قالوا: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: الوجوب بالذات، ممتاز عن الوجوب بالغير بقيد سلبي. وهو أنه لا يحصل معه القيد الزائد الحاصل في جانب الوجوب بالغير؟ قلنا: لأن الوجوب الذاتي، وهو القدر المشترك بين الصورتين إما أن يكون واجبا لذاته وإما أن يكون ممكنا لذاته. فإن كان الأول امتنع أن يصير جزءا من ماهية الوجوب بالغير، لأن ماهية الوجوب بالغير صفة عارضة لماهيات الممكنات، والعارض للشيء يكون عارضا لذلك الشيء بجميع أجزائه، فلو كان أحد أجزائه هو الوجوب الذي هو واجب الوجود لذاته، لزم أن يكون الواجب لذاته مفتقرا إلى غيره وهو محال. وأما إن قلنا: إن مسمى الوجوب الذي هو القدر المشترك بين الصورتين
The third aspect: In explaining that the necessity by itself cannot be an existent attribute: That is because the necessity by itself is a composite essence. So it is possible by itself. It follows that the necessity by itself is possible by itself, which is impossible. We said: The necessity by itself is a composite essence because the necessity by itself and the necessity by another share in the concept of being necessity, and it is distinguished from the other by being necessity by itself, and the other being necessity by another, and that by which they share is other than that by which they differ, so it follows that the necessity by itself is composed of these two conditions. If they say: Why is it not permissible to say that the necessity by itself is distinguished from the necessity by another by a negative condition, namely that it does not occur with it the additional condition that occurs in the aspect of the necessity by another? We say: Because the essential necessity, which is the common factor between the two forms, is either necessary by itself or possible by itself. If it is the first, it is impossible for it to become a part of the essence of the necessity by another, because the essence of the necessity by another is an accidental attribute of the essences of possible things, and the accidental attribute of a thing is accidental to that thing with all its parts. So if one of its parts were the necessity that is necessary of existence by itself, then it would be necessary for the necessary by itself to be dependent on another, which is impossible. And if we say that the meaning of necessity that is the common factor between the two forms...
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof that necessity by itself (wujub al-dhat) cannot be an existent attribute. The reason given is that wujub al-dhat would be a composite essence because it shares the concept of 'necessity' with wujub bil-ghayr (necessity by another) but differs by the condition 'by itself'. This composition implies contingency, which is impossible for the Necessary Being. The author then considers an objection: why not say that wujub al-dhat is distinguished from wujub bil-ghayr by a negative condition (i.e., the absence of the additional condition found in wujub bil-ghayr)? The response is that the common factor of 'necessity' itself is either necessary by itself or possible by itself. If it is necessary by itself, it cannot be part of the essence of wujub bil-ghayr, because that would make the Necessary Being dependent on another. If it is possible by itself, then wujub al-dhat would be contingent. The argument is left incomplete, but the direction is clear: the common factor cannot be part of a composite without contradiction.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that wujub al-dhat can be distinguished from wujub bil-ghayr by a simple negative condition (the absence of an extra condition), thus avoiding composition.
Correction: The response shows that even with a negative condition, the common factor of 'necessity' must be analyzed. If it is necessary by itself, it cannot be part of the composite wujub bil-ghayr without making the Necessary dependent on another. If it is possible, then wujub al-dhat becomes contingent. Thus, the negative condition does not save the argument.

Key Takeaway

The Author's Response: Mutual Cancellation of Doubts
Author's response: objections against wujub are countered by similar objections against imkan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the reality of wujub by arguing that objections against it also apply to imkan, thereby preserving the proofs for wujub.

Core Idea
Doubts against wujub cancel out when applied to imkan, leaving the proofs intact.

In this passage, the author presents his response to the objections raised against self-necessity (wujub). Instead of directly refuting each objection, he shows that the same doubts could be leveled against self-contingency (imkan). Since both sides are equally vulnerable, the positive proofs for wujub remain untouched. This is a powerful dialectical move that the lesson will unpack.

Guided Path
1
Set the context
Recall that previous sections raised objections against wujub being a positive reality. Now the author gives his answer (al-jawab). Understand that this is a dialectical rebuttal.
The author does not try to prove wujub from scratch; he uses the objections' own logic against them.
2
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the logical flow: premise, dichotomy, counter-argument, conclusion.
Notice the phrase 'la shakka anna' (there is no doubt that) which establishes certainty.
3
Identify the logical structure
Break down the argument into its components: (1) There is an existent. (2) It is either wajib or mumkin. (3) Your doubts against wujub apply equally to imkan. (4) Therefore, the doubts cancel out, and the proofs for wujub remain sound.
This is a classic argument by parity of reasoning.
4
Understand the dialectical strategy
The author uses a refutation by equivalence: if the same criticism undermines both sides, then neither side is refuted. Ask yourself: Why is this a strong move?
It avoids the need to answer each objection individually.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this argument preserves wujub as a real attribute. The author does not claim wujub is proven beyond all doubt; he only shows that the objections are not decisive.
This lesson shows a key principle in kalam: mutual cancellation of arguments leaves initial proofs standing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا جملة ما يذكر في هذا الباب . والجواب : أن نقول : لا شك أن في الوجود [موجودا] ولا شك أن ذلك الموجود إما أن يكون واجبا لذاته، وإما أن يكون ممكنا لذاته ، وهذه الشكوك التي ذكرتموها في الطعن في الوجوب بالذات ، معارضة بالشكوك المذكورة في الطعن في الإمكان الذاتي . ولما تعارضت هذه الشكوك ، بقي ما ذكرناه من الدلائل الدالة على أن الوجوب بالذات أمر حاصل متحقق سليما عن الطعن [ وبالله التوفيق] .
So this is the sum of what is mentioned in this chapter. And the answer: that we say: there is no doubt that in existence there is [an existent], and there is no doubt that that existent is either necessary by itself (wajib li-dhatih) or contingent by itself (mumkin li-dhatih). And these doubts (shukuk) which you have mentioned in the criticism (ta'n) of self-necessity (al-wujub bi-dhat) are countered by the doubts mentioned in the criticism of self-contingency (al-imkan al-dhati). And when these doubts are mutually opposed, what we have mentioned of proofs (dala'il) indicating that self-necessity is an actual realized matter remains safe from criticism. [And with God is success.]
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage provides the author's response (al-jawab) to the objections raised in the previous proofs against wujub being a positive reality. The response: (1) There is no doubt that an existent exists. (2) That existent is either necessary by itself (wajib li-dhatih) or contingent by itself (mumkin li-dhatih). (3) The doubts (shukuk) raised to attack self-necessity (al-wujub bi-dhat) are matched by equivalent doubts that could be raised against self-contingency (al-imkan al-dhati). (4) Since these doubts cancel each other out, the positive proofs (dala'il) that establish self-necessity as an actual reality remain sound and unaffected by the criticism. The author thus defends the reality of wujub by showing that the same line of attack would equally undermine imkan, which is untenable.

Correction: The author shows that the same objections apply equally to imkan, causing them to cancel out. Thus wujub remains rationally defensible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Three Possible Meanings of Existence for God and Creatures
Classification of possible meanings of existence when applied to God and possible beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three logical positions regarding the meaning of 'existence' when predicated of the Necessary Being and possible beings.

Core Idea
The term 'existence' can either be shared, an attribute, or self-subsistent.

Sanusi begins by noting a self-evident fact: we describe both God and creatures as 'existing'. But what does 'existence' mean in each case? This passage lays out the three possible answers: either the term is purely homonymous, or it has a common meaning that is either an attribute superadded to an essence or a self-subsistent reality. Understanding this framework is crucial for subsequent discussions on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the flow from the initial observation to the enumeration of three possibilities.
Pay attention to the logical connectors: 'إما... وإما'.
2
Identify the three divisions
The passage presents three possible positions: (1) verbal homonymy, (2) existence as an attribute of an essence, (3) existence as a self-subsistent reality. Trace how the author arrives at these.
The first division is between common concept and homonymy; the common concept further splits into two.
3
Understand the two sub-options under common concept
If 'existence' has a common meaning, then either it is a mere attribute (like 'white' to milk) or it is a self-subsistent reality (like the essence itself). Note the contrast.
This distinction is key to later arguments about God's attributes being identical to His essence.
4
Apply the framework
Consider how each position would affect our understanding of God's existence and attributes. The author doesn't decide yet, but you should see the logical options.
This classification is foundational for kalam discussions on divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثالثة
في
أن وجود الله - تعالى - نفس ماهيته أرصفة زائدة على ماهيه ؟
إنه من المعلوم بالضرورة أنا نصف واجب الوجود لذاته بأنه موجود، ونصف أيضا ممكن الوجود لذاته بأنه موجود. فنقول: إما أن يكون وقوع لفظ الوجود على هذين القسمين بحسب مفهوم مشترك بين هذين القسمين، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك بل يكون ذلك بحسب الاشتراك اللفظي فقط. فإذا كان الحق هو القسم الأول. فنقول: هذا القسم ينقسم إلى قسمين آخرين، وذلك لأن ذلك المفهوم المسمى بالوجود، إما أن يقال: إنه في حق واجب الوجود مقارن لماهية أخرى، ويكون هذا الوجود صفة لتلك الماهية ولاحقا من لواحقها، وإما أن يقال: إنه [أمر؟] قائم بنفسه مستقل بذاته من غير أن يكون صفة لشيء من الماهيات، ومن غير أن يكون عارضا لشيء من الحقائق.
فيثبت: أن القول في وجود الله تعالى لا يمكن أن يخرج عن هذه الأقسام الثلاثة.
The third issue: whether the existence of God – exalted – is identical to His essence or additional to His essence? It is known by necessity that we describe the necessary being in itself as existing, and we also describe the possible being in itself as existing. So we say: either the application of the word 'existence' to these two categories is according to a common concept shared between these two categories, or it is not so but rather that is according to mere verbal homonymy. If the truth is the first category, then we say: this category is divided into two further categories. This is because that concept called existence: either it is said that in the case of the necessary being it coexists with another essence, and this existence is an attribute of that essence and a concomitant of its concomitants, or it is said that it is a matter subsisting by itself, independent in its essence, without being an attribute of any essence, and without being an accident of any of the realities. So it is established that the statement concerning the existence of God cannot go beyond these three divisions.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage classifies the possible meanings of 'existence' when predicated of the Necessary Being and possible beings. The author begins by noting a self-evident fact: we use the term 'existence' for both. Then he proposes two broad possibilities: either the term has a common meaning, or it is purely homonymous. If it has a common meaning, then that common meaning either functions as an attribute superadded to an essence (as in creatures) or it is a self-subsistent reality (as in God). Thus, any view on the relationship between God's existence and His essence must fall into one of these three positions: homonymy, existence as attribute, or existence as self-subsistent. The author does not yet decide which is correct but establishes the logical framework.

The Initial Division: Homonymy or Common Concept?
Razi divides the term 'existence' into homonymy vs. common concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first division of opinions on whether the word 'existence' applies to God and creatures with a common meaning or only as a homonym, and the first view that it is homonymy.

Core Idea
'Existence' may be a homonym or a common concept; this shapes theology.

Before deciding whether God's existence is identical to His essence or additional, we must settle what 'existence' means when applied to God and creatures. Razi begins with a necessary fact: we say both the Necessary Being and contingent beings 'exist'. He then presents the logical options: either 'existence' has a shared concept or it is mere homonymy. In this lesson, we follow his classification up to the first view—pure homonymy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the translation. Note the opening question and the initial division into two possibilities.
The passage starts with the question and establishes a common ground.
2
Identify the two possibilities
Find where Razi says 'إما أن يكون وقوع لفظ الوجود على هذين القسمين بحسب مفهوم مشترك' and 'وإما أن لا يكون كذلك'. These are the two options: common concept or homonymy.
3
Understand what 'common concept' means
A common concept (مفهوم مشترك) means the word 'existence' has one meaning shared between God and creatures. Homonymy (اشتراك لفظي) means the word has different meanings.
This distinction is foundational for the subsequent debate.
4
Note the conclusion
Razi states that the truth is the first division (common concept). This sets the stage for further subdivision in the next lesson.
The passage ends with 'فإذا كان الحق هو القسم الأول'.

المسألة الثالثة في أن وجود الله - تعالى - نفس ماهيته أم صفة زائدة على ماهيته؟ إنه من المعلوم بالضرورة أنا نصف واجب الوجود لذاته بأنه موجود، ونصف أيضا ممكن الوجود لذاته بأنه موجود. فنقول: إما أن يكون وقوع لفظ الوجود على هذين القسمين بحسب مفهوم مشترك بين هذين القسمين، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك بل يكون ذلك بحسب الاشتراك اللفظي فقط، فإذا كان الحق هو القسم الأول. فنقول: هذا القسم ينقسم إلى قسمين آخرين، وذلك لأن ذلك المفهوم المسمى بالوجود، إما أن يقال: إنه في حق واجب الوجود مقارن لماهية أخرى، ويكون هذا الوجود صفة لتلك الماهية ولاحقا من لواحقها، وإما أن يقال: إنه أمر قائم بنفسه مستقل بذاته من غير أن يكون صفة لشيء من الماهيات ومن غير أن يكون عارضا لشيء من الحقائق. فيثبت: أن القول في وجود الله تعالى لا يمكن أن يخرج عن هذه الاقسام الثلاثة. فالأول قول من يقول: لفظ الموجود الواقع على الواجب لذاته، وعلى الممكن لذاته، لا يفيد مفهوما واحدا مشتركا فيه بين القسمين، بل هو بحسب الاشتراك اللفظي فقط. والثاني قول من يقول: لفظ الموجود يفيد مفهوما واحدا، إلا أنه في حق واجب الوجود لذاته، وجودا مجردا، أعني أنه وجود بشرط كونه غير عارض لشيء من الماهيات، بل يكون وجودا قائما بنفسه، وبهذا التقدير يكون وجود الله تعالى نفس حقيقته. والثالث: قول من يقول: إن الوجود صفة من صفات حقيقة الله تعالى، ونعت من نعوت ماهيته، وبهذا التقدير فوجود الله غير ماهيته. وهذه الأقوال الثلاثة قد ذهب إلى كل واحد منها عالم من الناس. فالأول: قول طائفة عظيمة من المتكلمين كأبي الحسن الأشعري، وأبي الحسين البصري. والثاني: هو القول الذي اختاره أبو علي بن سينا في جميع كتبه. والثالث: قول طائفة عظيمة من المتكلمين، وهو الذي نصرناه في أكثر كتبنا.
The Third Question: Is the existence of God, exalted, identical to His essence or an attribute additional to His essence? It is known necessarily that we describe the Necessary Existence in itself as existing, and we also describe the contingent existence in itself as existing. So we say: Either the application of the word 'existence' to these two types is according to a common concept between these two types, or it is not so but rather it is only according to verbal homonymy. If the truth is the first division, we say: This division divides into two other divisions, because that concept called existence, either it is said that with respect to the Necessary Existence it is concomitant with another essence, and this existence is an attribute of that essence and one of its accidents; or it is said that it is a thing subsistent by itself, independent in its essence, without being an attribute of any essence and without being an accident of any reality. So it is established that the statement about the existence of God most high cannot go beyond these three divisions. So the first is the statement of those who say: the word 'existent' applied to the Necessary in itself and to the contingent in itself does not yield a single common concept between the two types, but rather it is only according to verbal homonymy. The second is the statement of those who say: the word 'existent' yields a single concept, except that with respect to the Necessary Existence in itself, it is pure existence, meaning that it is existence conditioned by not being an accident of any essence, but rather it is existence subsisting by itself, and on this estimation the existence of God is identical to His essence. The third is the statement of those who say: existence is an attribute of the attributes of God's reality, and a description of His essence, and on this estimation the existence of God is other than His essence. And these three statements – each one of them has been adopted by a group of people. The first: the statement of a great party of theologians like Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri. The second: it is the statement chosen by Abu Ali ibn Sina in all his books. The third: the statement of a great party of theologians, and it is what we have supported in most of our books.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a classification and statement of the problem regarding the nature of God's existence. The author begins by stating the question: Is God's existence identical to His essence or additional to it? He establishes a necessary background: we describe both the Necessary Being and contingent beings as 'existing'. The key issue is whether the term 'existence' applies to both with a common meaning or not. The author divides into two initial possibilities: either there is a common concept, or there is only verbal homonymy. He then takes the first (common concept) as the truth and further divides it into two: (a) existence is an attribute of the essence, concomitant with it; or (b) existence is a self-subsistent entity, not an attribute of any essence. Thus we have three total views: (1) Verbal homonymy – 'existent' means different things when applied to God and creatures. (2) Self-subsistent existence – existence is a common concept but in God it is not an attribute; rather it is a pure, self-subsistent reality identical to His essence. (3) Attributive existence – existence is a real attribute distinct from God's essence. The author then names proponents: View 1 is held by many theologians like al-Ash'ari and al-Basri; View 2 is Ibn Sina's position; View 3 is held by many theologians and is the author's own view. This sets up the subsequent refutation of View 1.

Key Terms
وجود (wujud)
ماهية (mahiyya)
واجب الوجود (wajib al-wujud)
ممكن الوجود (mumkin al-wujud)
اشتراك لفظي (ishtirak lafzi)
مفهوم مشترك (mafhum mushtarak)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the homonymy view is obviously false and not worth considering.
Correction: Razi presents homonymy as a legitimate logical possibility, even though he chooses the common concept. It is a valid view held by some thinkers.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first division regarding the application of 'existence' to God and contingents?
A) Existence is an attribute of both.
B) Existence is either a common concept or a homonym.
C) Existence is identical to essence for God but not for contingents.
D) Existence is a mental construct.

Key Takeaway
The initial logical fork is whether 'existence' has a shared meaning or is a homonym; Razi affirms a common concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah exists' and 'I exist,' do you feel a difference in the kind of existence? How does that feeling align with Razi's claim of a common concept?
?
If 'existence' truly has a single meaning for both God and you, what does that mean for how you understand your own life? Are you ready to let that common concept draw you closer to the reality of the Necessary?
?
You affirm God's existence daily in prayer. But have you ever pondered what that word 'existence' actually means when applied to Him? What would change in your faith if you realized it was just a homonym?

Two Sub-Views of the Common Concept: Essential vs. Attributive Existence
Razi subdivides the common concept view into attributive vs. essential existence, yielding three total views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two sub-views that arise from the common concept position—whether existence is an attribute of essence or a self-subsistent reality—and the complete set of three views on God's existence.

Core Idea
If 'existence' is a common concept, it either attaches to essence or stands alone as God's essence.

Having established that the truth lies in the common concept view, Razi now unpacks it further. The common concept of existence can either be an attribute of an essence (like whiteness for a white object) or a self-subsistent reality (not an attribute at all). This leads to two additional views, making three total. In this lesson, we explore these two positions and see who held each view, including Razi's own stance.

Guided Path
1
Read the remainder of the passage
Starting from 'فنقول: هذا القسم ينقسم إلى قسمين آخرين', read through to the end. Notice how the common concept is subdivided.
2
Identify the two sub-divisions
Find the two possibilities: existence as an attribute concomitant with an essence, or existence as a self-subsistent entity independent of any essence.
These are the second and third views when combined with the first (homonymy).
3
Match each view to its proponents
Note that View 1 (homonymy) is held by al-Ash'ari and al-Basri, View 2 (self-subsistent existence) by Ibn Sina, and View 3 (existence as attribute) by many theologians including Razi himself.
This shows the historical significance of each position.
4
Visualize the trichotomy
The three views are mutually exclusive and exhaustive according to Razi. Draw a diagram: First divide into common/homonymy, then common divides into attribute vs. self-subsistent.

The Third Question: Is the existence of God, exalted, identical to His essence or an attribute additional to His essence? Correction: They differ crucially: in View 2, existence is not an attribute at all (it is the essence itself), while in View 3, existence is a real attribute distinct from the essence.

A) The homonymy view.

D) The view that existence is a mental construct.

The common concept view splits into two, yielding three total views on God's existence; Razi favors the attributive view.

?

Three Proofs Against Verbal Homonymy of Existence
Author presents three proofs against the view that existence is merely a verbal homonym.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three proofs against the verbal homonymy view of existence and why they demonstrate that existence is a single, shared concept.

Core Idea
Existence cannot be a mere label; three rational proofs show it must be a single shared concept.

The author begins a crucial argument: is the term 'existent' merely a homonym? This view would undermine any shared understanding of Allah's existence and creation's existence. The author presents three proofs to show that this cannot be the case, starting from the very structure of reason.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recall that the author earlier divided statements about existence into three views. This passage refutes the first view, that 'existent' is applied by verbal homonymy only.
The other two views (univocal and analogical) are not discussed here.
2
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation. Notice the author's structure: an introduction and then three proofs.
The author says he repeats proofs from earlier discussions.
3
Examine the first proof
The first proof: existence and non-existence are opposites. For opposition to hold, each must be a single concept. If existence had multiple unrelated meanings, it could not uniformly oppose non-existence.
This proof is based on the principle of contradiction.
4
Examine the second proof
The second proof: existence can be divided into necessary, contingent, substance, accident. The term being divided must have a common meaning across the divisions, otherwise the division would be incoherent.
A term like 'bank' (financial vs. riverbank) cannot be divided into meaningful subcategories.
5
Examine the third proof
The third proof: believing something is existent does not conflict with believing it has other attributes (necessary, contingent, etc.). This compatibility shows that 'existent' carries a shared core meaning.
If 'existent' were purely homonymous, such compatibility would be coincidental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب على العاقل أن يتأمل في هذا التقسيم الذي ذكرناه حتى يعلم يقينا أن هذه الأقوال الثلاثة لا مزيد عليها البتة. ونقول: أما القول الأول وهو أن لفظ الموجود واقع على الواجب لذاته، وعلى الممكن لذاته، لا بحسب مفهوم واحد، بل بحسب الاشتراك اللفظي فقط. فنقول: إنا قد دللنا في مسائل الوجود على فساده، ولا بأس بإعادة بعض تلك الوجوه في هذا المقام، فنقول: الذي يدل على فساده وجوه: الأول: إن بديهة العقل حاكمة بأن الوجود لا يقابله إلا العدم، وإن العدم لا يقابله إلا الوجود، فوجب أن يكون الوجود مفهوما واحدا، كما أن العدم مفهوم واحد، حتى يصح ذلك التقابل بينهما. الثاني: إن الوجود يصح تقسيمه إلى الواجب، وإلى الممكن والجوهر والعرض، ومورد التقسيم مشترك بين كل الأقسام. الثالث: إن اعتقاد كونه موجودا، لا ينافيه اعتقاد كونه واجبا أو ممكنا أو جوهرا أو عرضا، فوجب أن يكون المفهوم من كونه موجودا فيه قدر مشترك بين الكل.
It is obligatory for the intelligent to contemplate this division we have mentioned, so that he knows with certainty that there is absolutely no addition to these three statements. And we say: As for the first statement, namely that the word 'existent' is applied to the Necessary in itself and to the contingent in itself not according to a single concept but only according to verbal homonymy, we say: We have demonstrated in the Questions of Existence its corruption, and there is no harm in repeating some of those proofs in this context. So we say: That which indicates its corruption are proofs: First: The intuition of reason judges that nothing opposes existence except non-existence, and nothing opposes non-existence except existence, so it is necessary that existence be a single concept, just as non-existence is a single concept, so that the opposition between them is valid. Second: Existence can be divided into the necessary, the contingent, substance and accident, and the domain of division is common to all parts. Third: The belief that something is existing does not contradict the belief that it is necessary or possible or substance or accident, so it is necessary that the concept of its being existent has a common element among all.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the first view – that existence is a mere verbal homonym. The author begins by urging the intelligent person to reflect on the exhaustive division, confirming that the three views cover all possibilities. Then he addresses the first view specifically. He mentions that he has already proven its corruption elsewhere but repeats some proofs here. Three proofs are given: (1) The opposition between existence and non-existence requires that each be a single concept. If 'existence' had multiple unrelated meanings, it could not uniformly oppose non-existence. (2) Existence can be divided into kinds (necessary, contingent, substance, accident). The term being divided must have a common meaning across the divisions. (3) Believing something is existent does not conflict with believing it has other properties (like necessity or contingency). This compatibility indicates that 'existent' conveys a shared core meaning. Thus, the view of mere verbal homonymy is shown to be false.

Key Terms
اشتراك لفظي (ishtirak lafzi)
بديهة العقل (badīhat al-'aql)
تقابل (taqābul)
تقسيم (taqsīm)
مورد التقسيم (mawrid al-taqsīm)
قدر مشترك (qadr mushtarak)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three proofs are independent and do not rely on a common principle.
Correction: All three proofs are based on the principle that a term with a single opposite or domain of division must have a single meaning. They are not independent but reinforce the same conclusion.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof against verbal homonymy?
A) The division of existence into necessary and contingent.
B) The opposition of existence to non-existence requires a single concept.
C) The belief in existence is compatible with other attributes.
D) The word 'existent' is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The three proofs together show that existence must be a single concept, refuting the view of mere verbal homonymy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah's existence and your existence are the same type of existence? If so, how does that affect your daily awareness of Him?
?
When you say 'Allah is existent,' do you feel the weight of that statement as more than just a label? How can you make that belief transform your actions?
?
If existence were merely a verbal homonym, your faith would be empty jargon. Does your heart tremble at that thought? What changes can you make today to ensure your belief is grounded in reality?

Al-Razi's Argument: Existence is One Concept
Al-Razi argues that existence is a single concept because conceptual distinction needs an external differentiator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that existence is a single concept, distinguished only by external attributes.

Core Idea
Existence is one; distinctions come from outside.

Al-Razi presents a clever argument: if necessary and possible existence were different in themselves, reason would distinguish them immediately, but it doesn't. This shows existence itself is a single shared concept, and any distinction comes from an external factor.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow: analogy, conditional, conclusion.
The analogy with blackness and whiteness is key.
2
Understand the analogy
Al-Razi says we distinguish blackness and whiteness by their concepts alone. This is the model for how distinct concepts behave.
3
Follow the conditional argument
If existence of Necessary and existence of possible were distinct concepts, reason would distinguish them immediately. But it does not—only after adding external attribution. So they are not distinct in themselves.
Pay attention to the phrase 'فلو كان ... لكن من المعلوم بالضرورة أنه ليس كذلك'.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Existence as such is a single concept. Existents are only distinguished by something external to their essence.
This is the main takeaway: existence is univocal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : نحن إذا عقلنا معنى السواد ومعنى البياض، قضى العقل بامتياز معنى السواد عن معنى البياض بنفسه من غير أن يحتاج فيه إلى إلحاق قيد آخر، بهما يوجب الامتياز. فلو كان وجود الواجب، حقيقة مخالفة لوجود الممكن، لكنا إذا تصورنا هذين الوجودين، وجب أن تحكم بديهة العقل بامتياز أحدهما عن الآخر، لكن من المعلوم بالضرورة أنه ليس كذلك، فإنا ما لم نصف أحد الموجودين إلى الواجب، والموجود الآخر إلى الممكن، لم يحصل في العقل امتياز أحد الموجودين عن الآخر. فعلمنا أن الموجودات في أنفسها ليست متمايزة من حيث إنها وجودات، بل الوجود من حيث إنه وجود، مفهوم واحد، وأنه إنما يمتاز بعضها عن بعض بسبب خارج عن ماهيتها.
The seventh: When we conceive the meaning of blackness and the meaning of whiteness, reason judges the distinction of the meaning of blackness from the meaning of whiteness by itself without needing to attach another predicate that would cause distinction. So if the existence of the Necessary were a reality different from the existence of the possible, then when we conceive these two existences, the innate reason would necessarily judge the distinction of one from the other. But it is known necessarily that it is not so, for unless we attribute one of the two existents to the Necessary and the other existent to the possible, no distinction of one existent from the other occurs in the mind. Thus we know that existents in themselves are not distinct insofar as they are existences, rather existence insofar as it is existence is a single concept, and that they are only distinguished from one another by something external to their essence.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof that existence is a single concept. Al-Razi (the author) argues by analogy: when we conceive blackness and whiteness, our innate reason distinguishes them immediately without needing an external qualifier. If existence of the Necessary Being (wajib) and existence of the possible (mumkin) were different realities, then upon conceiving both, reason would naturally distinguish them. But experience shows this is not the case: we only distinguish the two existents when we additionally attribute one to the Necessary and the other to the possible. Therefore, the distinction between existents does not come from existence itself, but from something external to their essence. Hence existence is a single shared concept.

Key Terms
وجود (wujud)
واجب (wajib)
ممكن (mumkin)
بديهة العقل (badihah al-'aql)
امتياز (imtiyaz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the distinction between necessary and possible existence implies different meanings of 'existence'.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the distinction is due to external attributes, not existence itself, so 'existence' is a single concept.

Checkpoint
According to the seventh argument, why do we not immediately distinguish between the existence of the Necessary and the existence of the possible?
A) Because they are identical in essence
B) Because our innate reason does not operate on such abstract concepts
C) Because we need an external attribution to distinguish them
D) Because existence is not a real concept

Key Takeaway
Existence is a single concept; the distinction between necessary and possible existence is due to external attributes, not existence itself.

The Eighth Proof: Consistency of the Need for a Creator
Al-Razi argues that the concept of existence remains unchanged regardless of the nature of the creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi argues that the constancy of the need for a creator, regardless of the creator's nature, demonstrates that existence is a single concept.

Core Idea
The need for a creator is uniform, so existence is one.

Al-Razi presents a unique argument: after establishing that the world needs a creator, he examines different possible natures of that creator. Surprisingly, the fundamental belief that the world requires a creator remains unchanged across all scenarios. This constancy reveals something profound about the concept of existence itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and translation. Notice the logical flow from the established proof that the world is originated to the various assumptions about the creator.
2
Identify the assumptions
List the different possibilities Al-Razi considers about the creator: eternal or originated, necessary by itself or by cause, body or accident or neither.
These are the 'all divisions' he refers to.
3
Observe the constant
Despite the different assumptions, the belief that the world needs a creator remains constant and unchanged. Ask yourself why this constancy is significant.
4
Connect to existence
Understand the conclusion: this constancy indicates that existence itself is a single meaning shared by all the different possibilities. The concept of being an existent in external entities is one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن : إنا إذا أقمنا الدليل على أن العالم محدث، قضى العقل بافتقاره إلى موجود يوجده، ثم بعد ذلك يقع البحث في أن ذلك الموجود الذي أوجد العالم، هل هو قديم أو محدث؟ وبتقدير أن يكون قديما، فهل هو واجب لذاته، أو واجب بسبب وجوب علته؟ وهل هو جسم أو حال في الجسم؟ أو لا جسم ولا حال في الجسم؟ وعلى جميع التقديرات فإن اعتقاد أن العالم لا بد له من موجد يوجده، ومن كائن يكونه، باقي ثابت غير متغير، وكل ذلك يدل على أن المعقول من كونه موجودا متحققا في الأعيان: أمر واحد مشترك فيه بين كل الأقسام.
The eighth: When we establish the proof that the world is originated, reason judges its need for an existent that brings it into existence. Then after that, inquiry occurs concerning that existent that brought the world into existence: is it eternal or originated? And if it is assumed to be eternal, is it necessary by itself, or necessary due to the necessity of its cause? And is it a body or an accident in a body, or neither a body nor an accident? In all these assumptions, the belief that the world must have a creator that creates it and a maker that makes it remains constant and unchanged. And all that indicates that the intelligible from its being an existent realized in external entities is a single matter shared among all divisions.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof. Al-Razi begins with the established proof that the world is originated (muhdath), which entails its need for a creator (mujid). Then various possibilities about that creator are considered: is it eternal or originated? If eternal, is it necessary by itself or by another? Is it a body, an accident, or neither? Regardless of which assumption is made, the core belief that the world requires a creator remains constant and unchanged. This constancy of the need for a creator, whatever its nature, shows that the concept of 'being an existent realized in external entities' is a single meaning common to all those divisions. Thus, existence is one concept.

Key Terms
عالَم
محدَث
موجِد
قديم
واجب لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Different possible natures of the creator imply different concepts of existence.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the need for a creator is uniform across all possibilities, indicating that existence itself is a single concept shared by all.

Checkpoint

A) The creator's necessity by itself
B) The world's need for a creator
C) The creator's being a body
D) The creator's being eternal

Key Takeaway
The need for a creator is consistent across all assumptions, proving that existence is a single concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imagine your certainty in God's existence was tested by doubts about His nature. Would your faith remain constant, as Al-Razi says the need for a creator is constant? What would that require of you today?
?
If the concept of existence is truly one, then your own existence is not separate from the Creator's. Does that thought change how you view your daily actions and your accountability to Him?
?
You must ask yourself: Is your belief in God dependent on your intellectual assumptions, or is it as unshakable as the proof that the world needs a creator? What step will you take to anchor your faith in that constant need?

The Ninth Proof: Existence is a Single Concept
Al-Razi argues that existence is a single concept by showing it is compatible with contradictory attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that existence is a single concept because it does not contradict any specific attribute.

Core Idea
Existence remains the same across contradictory attributes.

In this proof, Al-Razi uses a simple observation about our concepts: the idea of 'existence' does not clash with any particular attribute, even when those attributes contradict each other. This shows that existence is a distinct, single concept underlying all things. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage slowly once. Notice how Al-Razi sets up two pairs of contradictory concepts before drawing his conclusion.
The pairs are: necessary of existence vs possible of existence, and body occupying space vs immaterial existent.
2
Identify the contradictory pairs
List the two pairs of attributes that Al-Razi calls contradictory. Understand why each pair cannot be true of the same thing at the same time.
Necessary and possible are exclusive; material and immaterial are exclusive.
3
Observe the neutrality of existence
Notice that Al-Razi says 'being an existent realized in external entities' does not contradict any of these pairs. It can be combined with each one.
This neutrality is the key to the argument: existence is a separate concept that is always compatible.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Al-Razi concludes that existence is a single concept (mafhum wahid) that does not vary with different forms. Appreciate why this matters for understanding the nature of existence.
This proof argues against those who think existence is multiple or ambiguous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع : إن بديهة العقل شاهدة بأن المعقول من كونه واجب الوجود لذاته ينافي المعقول من كونه ممكن الوجود لذاته وبالعكس. وأيضا المعقول من كونه جسما متحيزا، ينافي المعقول من كونه موجودا مجردا، فجميع هذه الاعتبارات متناقضة متغايرة، وصريح العقل شاهد بأن المعقول من كونه موجودا محصلا في الأعيان، لا يناقض شيئا من هذه الاعتبارات ولا ينافيها. وذلك يدل على أن كونه موجودا، مفهوم مغاير لهذه الاعتبارات المخصوصة باقي مع كل واحد منها. وذلك يدل على أن المفهوم من كونه موجودا مفهوما واحدا لا يختلف باختلاف هذه الصور.
The ninth: Innate reason witnesses that the intelligible of being necessary of existence by itself is contrary to the intelligible of being possible of existence by itself and vice versa. Also, the intelligible of being a body occupying space is contrary to the intelligible of being an immaterial existent. So all these considerations are contradictory and different, and plain reason witnesses that the intelligible of being an existent realized in external entities does not contradict any of these considerations nor is it contrary to them. That indicates that 'being existent' is a concept different from these specific considerations and remains with each one of them. And that indicates that the concept of 'being existent' is a single concept that does not differ with the difference of these forms.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth proof. Al-Razi notes that innate reason tells us that the concept of 'necessary of existence by itself' contradicts 'possible of existence by itself'. Similarly, being a body occupying space contradicts being immaterial. These specific considerations are contradictory. However, the concept of 'being an existent realized in external entities' does not contradict any of these contradictory considerations; it remains compatible with all. Therefore, the meaning of existence is distinct from these specific attributes and remains constant across them. Hence, existence is a single concept that does not vary with varying forms.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود (necessary of existence)
ممكن الوجود (possible of existence)
جسم متحيز (body occupying space)
مجرد (immaterial)
مفهوم (concept)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think existence is different for each kind of thing (e.g., God's existence is different from a human's).
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the concept of existence is the same across all attributes; it does not change even when the attributes are contradictory.

Checkpoint
What does Al-Razi say about the concept of 'being an existent' in relation to contradictory specific attributes?
A) It contradicts them
B) It is identical to one of them
C) It does not contradict any of them
D) It cancels them out

Key Takeaway
Existence is a single, neutral concept that does not contradict any specific attribute, proving its unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence is a single concept shared by the Creator and the creature, does that make you feel closer to Allah or more aware of the vast difference between His necessary existence and your possible one? How does this realization change your daily awareness of Him?
?
Al-Razi shows that existence remains constant across contradictory attributes. Where in your life are you tying your identity and security to attributes (like health, wealth, or status) instead of the sheer gift of existence? What would it look like to live today with the conviction that your existence is a neutral gift from the Necessary Existent?
?
The proof argues that existence is not opposed to anything. Yet your heart often opposes the trials that come with your existence. How can you surrender to the fact that your existence itself is a sign of Allah's will, independent of the circumstances? Will you commit to thanking Him not just for what you have, but for the very fact that you are?
