Refuting Four Possible Relationships Between Existence and Necessity
Sanusi refutes four possible relationships between existence and necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes all four conceivable relationships between existence and necessary existence, proving that they are distinct but inseparable.

Core Idea
Existence and necessity can be neither cause nor effect nor both effects of a third thing nor unrelated.

After establishing the conceptual distinction, Sanusi enumerates four possible ways to relate existence and necessary existence. Each possibility is examined and shown to lead to a contradiction or absurdity. The only remaining conclusion is that the concepts are distinct but inseparable in God. This refutes the claim that the Necessary Existent is nothing but bare existence.

Guided Path
1
List the four divisions
Read the passage and identify the four possible relationships. Write them down in your own words.
The four are: (1) necessity entails existence, (2) existence entails necessity, (3) both entail a third, (4) neither entails the other and no third.
2
Examine the first division
Why does Sanusi reject the idea that necessity entails existence?
It would make existence an accident, contradicting the premise.
3
Examine the second division
Why is the idea that existence entails necessity false?
Shared cannot entail unique.
4
Examine the third and fourth divisions
Understand the refutations for the third (a third quiddity) and fourth (no relation).
Both lead to contradictions.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
All four are false. What does this imply about the relationship between existence and necessity?
They are distinct but necessarily coextensive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إنا قد دللنا على أن لفظ الموجود واقع على الواجب وعلى الممكن ، بحسب مفهوم واحد . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إما أن يكون المعقول من كونه تعالى موجودا ، والمعقول من كونه تعالى واجبا لذاته : أمر واحد من غير تغاير البتة ، وإما أن يكون المعقول من كل واحد منهما مغايرا للمعقول من الآخر. والأول باطل ، لأنه لما كان كونه واجبا لذاته غير مشترك فيه وبينه وبين الممكنات ، وجب أن لا يكون كونه موجودا أمرا مشتركا فيه بينه وبين الممكنات والوجوب الذاتي غير مشترك فيه بينه وبين الممكنات ، فوجب القطع بأن كونه موجودا مغايرا لكونه واجبا ، وإذا ثبت هذا التغاير فنقول : إما أن يقال : الوجوب بالذات يستلزم الوجود أو يقال بالعكس منه وهو أن الوجود يستلزم الوجوب بالذات ، أو يقال لا هذا يستلزم ذاك ولا بالعكس ، ولكن حصلت ماهية ثالثة . وتلك الماهية تكون مستلزمة لهذين القيدين ، وإما أن يقال : لا هذا يستلزم ذاك ولا ذالك يستلزم هذا ، وليس هناك ماهية ثالثة تستلزمهما معا . فهذه أقسام أربعة لا مزيد عليها .
أما القسم الأول : وهو أن يقال : الوجوب بالذات يستلزم الوجود . فعلى هذا التقدير ، يكون الوجود عارضا للوجوب بالذات . وقد فرضنا ذلك الوجود، غير عارض بشيء من الماهيات . هذا خلف
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : الوجود يستلزم الوجوب بالذات ، فهذا فاسد لأن مسمى الوجود هو القدر المشترك بينه وبين الممكنات ، وما به المشاركة يمتنع أن يستلزم ما به الممايزة ، لأن الأشياء المتساوية في النوع، يمتنع أن تلزمها لوازم مختلفة بالنوع
وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يقال : حصل هناك مفهوم ثالث ، يستلزم هذين القيدين . فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير يكون الوجوب بالذات ويكون الوجود عارضين لتلك الماهية. وقد فرضنا أنه ليس كذلك . هذا خلف
وأما القسم الرابع : وهو أن لا يكون واحدا منهما مستلزما للآخر ، وليس هناك ثالث يستلزمهما ، فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير لا يكون ذلك الوجود مستلزما للوجوب الذاتي ، بل يكون ذلك الوجود عارضا مفارقا لذلك الوجود ، فحينئذ لا يكون الواجب لذاته، واجبا لذاته ، بل ممكنا لذاته ، وذلك باطل . فثبت بهذا البرهان : أن القول بأن واجب الوجود لذاته لا حقيقة له إلا الوجود المجرد . كلام باطل فاسد
The fourth proof: We have already demonstrated that the word 'existent' is applied to the Necessary and to the possible according to one concept. When this is established, we say: either the intelligible of His being existent and the intelligible of His being necessary by itself are one thing without any difference, or the intelligible of each one is different from the intelligible of the other. The first is false, because since His being necessary by itself is not shared between Him and the possible beings, it must be that His being existent is not a matter shared between Him and the possible beings; and the self-necessity is not shared between Him and the possible beings, so it is necessary to judge that His being existent is different from His being necessary. When this difference is established, we say: either it is said that necessity by itself entails existence, or the reverse, that existence entails necessity by itself, or it is said that neither this entails that nor the reverse, but a third quiddity occurs, and that quiddity entails these two qualifications, or it is said that neither this entails that nor that entails this, and there is no third quiddity that entails both together. These are four divisions without addition.
As for the first division: that necessity by itself entails existence. On this assumption, existence would be an accident to necessity by itself. And we have assumed that existence is not an accident to any quiddity. This is a contradiction.
As for the second division: that existence entails necessity by itself; this is corrupt because the meaning of existence is the common factor between Him and the possible beings, and that which is shared cannot entail that which differentiates, because things equal in species cannot be attended by different necessary accidents in species.
As for the third division: that a third concept occurs which entails these two qualifications. We say: on this assumption, necessity by itself and existence would be accidents to that quiddity. And we have assumed that it is not so. This is a contradiction.
As for the fourth division: that neither one entails the other, and there is no third that entails them both; we say: on this assumption, that existence would not entail self-necessity, but rather that existence would be a separate accident to that existence, so then the necessary by itself would not be necessary by itself, but possible by itself, and that is false. Thus it is established by this proof that the statement that the Necessary Existent by itself has no reality except bare existence is a corrupt false statement.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument, which is a formal demonstration that the concept of existence (wujud) is distinct from the concept of necessary existence (wujub bi al-dhat). The proof proceeds in stages. First, it establishes that the term 'existent' is used univocally for both God and contingent beings. Then it considers two possibilities: either the concepts of existence and necessary existence are identical or they are different. The first is rejected because necessary existence is unique to God and not shared, while existence is shared; so they must be different. Having established difference, the author then enumerates four possible ways to relate the two concepts: (1) necessity entails existence, (2) existence entails necessity, (3) a third concept entails both, (4) neither entails the other and no third concept. Each is refuted: (1) would make existence an accident of necessity, contradicting the premise that existence is not an accident; (2) would require a shared property to entail a unique property, which is absurd; (3) would make both existence and necessity accidents of a third quiddity, again contradicting the premise; (4) would make existence a separate accident, making God contingent. Therefore, all four relations are impossible, proving that the claim that the Necessary Existent is nothing but bare existence is false.

Key Terms
الوجود
الوجوب بالذات
الاستلزام
الماهية
العارض
الخلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the refutation of the four divisions implies that existence and necessity are identical.
Correction: The refutation shows they are distinct but inseparable; they cannot be causally related or unrelated, but they are necessarily together.

Checkpoint
Why is the second division (existence entails necessity by itself) considered false?
A) Because it makes existence an accident of necessity.
B) Because a shared property cannot entail a unique property.
C) Because it implies a third quiddity that is impossible.
D) Because it makes God's existence separate from His necessity.

Key Takeaway
All four possible relationships between existence and necessity are impossible, proving they are distinct but inseparable in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's existence and necessity are inseparable, what does that mean for how you approach Him in your daily life? Are you treating Him as a concept to be analyzed or as a Reality that demands your entire being?
?
This proof shows that God cannot be reduced to 'bare existence' or any mental category. Have you been guilty of reducing God to a comfortable idea that fits your logic, rather than submitting to His absolute reality? What will you do today to repent of that intellectual arrogance?
?
Sanusi's argument forces you to either accept that God is necessary in every way or reject Him entirely. Where in your heart do you still try to make God 'optional'—convenient when needed but dismissed when inconvenient? How will you change that starting now?

Fifth Argument: Three Possibilities for God's Being the Origin
Begins argument that God's being the origin of contingent beings must be due to a qualification beyond bare existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classificatory premise of the fifth argument for God's oneness: that God's being the origin of contingent beings must be due to either His bare existence, a negative qualification, or a positive qualification, and why the first possibility is rejected.

Core Idea
Being the origin requires more than just existence — it demands a specific qualification.

In this fifth argument, the author examines why God is the origin of all possible beings. He proposes three exhaustive possibilities and begins to eliminate them. The passage we have sets up the classification and rejects the first option. As you read, notice the logical structure: the argument proceeds by elimination.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three possibilities
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the three options: (1) due to His mere existence, (2) due to existence with a negative qualification, (3) due to existence with a positive qualification. Understand each.
The text uses 'قيد سلبي' and 'قيد وجودي' for negative and positive qualifications.
2
Understand the rejection of the first
The text says 'والأول باطل' — the first is false. The reason given is that if mere existence were sufficient, then every existence would be an origin, which is not the case. Think about why that is a problem.
The argument is cut off, but the implication is that only God is the origin, not every existing thing.
3
Recognize the incomplete argument
Note that the passage ends with 'وإلا لوجب أن يكون كل...' indicating the proof continues. This lesson only covers the initial classification and rejection of the first option; the conclusion and handling of the other two options come later.
This is a common feature in classical texts — arguments span across pages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : لا شك أنه تعالى مبدءا لوجود الممكنات ، وكونه مبدءا لها : إما أن يكون لنفس كونه وجودا ، أو لنفس كونه وجودا مع قيد سلبي ، أو لنفس كونه وجودا مع قيد وجودي ، والأول باطل . وإلا لوجب أن يكون كل
The fifth proof: There is no doubt that He, exalted, is the origin of the existence of possible beings. And His being the origin of them: either it is due to His being existence itself, or due to His being existence with a negative qualification, or due to His being existence with a positive qualification. The first is false, because otherwise it would be necessary that every...
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage begins the fifth argument, which focuses on God's role as the origin (mabda') of contingent beings. The author first states that God is undoubtedly the origin of the existence of possible beings. He then classifies three possible explanations for why God is the origin: (1) it is due to His being mere existence, (2) due to His being existence with a negative qualification (i.e., a privative condition), or (3) due to His being existence with a positive qualification (i.e., an additional attribute). He rejects the first possibility, arguing that if mere existence were sufficient to be an origin, then every existence would be an origin of possible beings, which is false. The argument is incomplete here; the refutation of the other two possibilities and the conclusion are missing due to the page break.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's bare existence is sufficient to explain why He creates, since He is the necessary being.
Correction: The text argues that if mere existence were enough, then every existing thing (which exists) would be a creator, which is false. Therefore, some additional qualification is needed.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth argument, which possibility for God's being the origin is rejected?
A) Due to His being existence with a negative qualification.
B) Due to His being existence itself.
C) Due to His being existence with a positive qualification.
D) None; all are accepted.

Key Takeaway
God's mere existence cannot be sufficient to make Him the origin of contingent beings; some additional qualification is needed, which the argument then examines.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's mere existence were the reason He is the origin, then every existent would be an origin. Does your belief in God's existence truly distinguish Him from all other things, or do you sometimes treat His existence as just another fact?
?
The argument requires a positive qualification for God to be the origin. What specific attribute of God (e.g., power, will, wisdom) do you rely on when you acknowledge Him as your Creator? Are you living as if that attribute alone gives Him the right to your worship?
?
Consider the absurdity that if existence alone caused origination, everything would be a creator. How does this reshape your view of God's uniqueness? Do you actively recognize that He is not just one more existent, but the sole origin of all things?

The Four Divisions: First Two Possibilities
The author enumerates the first two logical possibilities and refutes them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two logical possibilities for the relationship between necessity-by-essence and existence in the Necessary Being, and why they lead to contradictions.

Core Idea
The relationship between necessity and existence cannot be simple entailment.

In this passage, the author begins a rigorous proof by exhaustively listing all possible logical relationships between necessity-by-essence and existence. He identifies four divisions with no increase. We will examine the first two: that necessity entails existence, and that existence entails necessity. Each is shown to be impossible under the assumption that the Necessary Being has no quiddity beyond existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening lines
Read the Arabic text from the beginning through the end of the second division. Observe how the author sets up the exhaustive classification.
The phrase 'لا مزيد عليها' emphasizes that these are all possible cases.
2
Understand the first division
The first division posits that necessity-by-essence entails existence. This would make existence an accident (عارض) upon necessity, contradicting the assumption that existence does not occur as an accident upon any quiddity.
The term 'خلف' means contradiction or logical inconsistency.
3
Understand the second division
The second division posits that existence entails necessity-by-essence. But existence is a common factor between the Necessary and contingent beings. A common factor cannot entail a differentiating factor, as things equal in species cannot have differing entailments.
This argument relies on the principle that what is shared cannot be the cause of what is unique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يقال : لا هذا يستلزم ذاك ولا ذالك يستلزم هذا ، وليس هناك ماهية ثالثة تستلزمهما معا . فهذه أقسام أربعة لا مزيد عليها .
أما القسم الأول : وهو أن يقال : الوجوب بالذات يستلزم الوجود . فعلى هذا التقدير ، يكون الوجود عارضا للوجوب بالذات . وقد فرضنا ذلك الوجود، غير عارض بشيء من الماهيات . هذا خلف
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : الوجود يستلزم الوجوب بالذات ، فهذا فاسد لأن مسمى الوجود هو القدر المشترك بينه وبين الممكنات ، وما به المشاركة يمتنع أن يستلزم ما به الممايزة ، لأن الأشياء المتساوية في النوع، يمتنع أن تلزمها لوازم مختلفة بالنوع
وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يقال : حصل هناك مفهوم ثالث ، يستلزم هذين القيدين . فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير يكون الوجوب بالذات ويكون الوجود عارضين لتلك الماهية. وقد فرضنا أنه ليس كذلك . هذا خلف
وأما القسم الرابع : وهو أن لا يكون واحدا منهما مستلزما للآخر ، وليس هناك ثالث يستلزمهما ، فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير لا يكون ذلك الوجود مستلزما للوجوب الذاتي ، بل يكون ذلك الوجود عارضا مفارقا لذلك الوجود ، فحينئذ لا يكون الواجب لذاته، واجبا لذاته ، بل ممكنا لذاته ، وذلك باطل . فثبت بهذا البرهان : أن القول بأن واجب الوجود لذاته لا حقيقة له إلا الوجود المجرد . كلام باطل فاسد
It can be said: neither does this entail that, nor does that entail this, and there is no third quiddity that entails both simultaneously. So these are four divisions with no increase.
As for the first division: it is to say: necessity-by-essence (al-wujūb bi al-dhāt) entails existence (al-wujūd). On this assumption, existence becomes an accident upon necessity-by-essence. And we have supposed that existence does not occur as an accident upon any quiddity. This is a contradiction.
As for the second division: it is to say: existence entails necessity-by-essence. This is invalid because the denotation of existence is the common factor between it and contingent things, and that by which there is participation cannot entail that by which there is differentiation, because things equal in species cannot have differing entailments by species.
As for the third division: it is to say: there occurs a third concept that entails these two qualifications. So we say: on this assumption, both necessity-by-essence and existence would be accidents upon that quiddity. And we have supposed it is not so. This is a contradiction.
As for the fourth division: it is that neither entails the other, and there is no third that entails them both. So we say: on this assumption, that existence is not entailing essential necessity; rather that existence is a separate accident upon that existence. Then the Necessary-by-essence would not be necessary-by-essence, but rather contingent-by-essence, and that is false. Thus it is established by this proof that the statement that the Necessary Being by essence has no reality except pure existence is a false, invalid statement.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhān) refuting the claim that the Necessary Being has no reality except pure existence. The author presents four logical possibilities for the relationship between necessity-by-essence (al-wujūb bi al-dhāt) and existence (al-wujūd): (1) necessity entails existence, (2) existence entails necessity, (3) a third concept entails both, (4) neither entails the other. Each possibility is examined and shown to lead to a contradiction under the assumption that the Necessary Being has no quiddity other than existence. For the first, existence would be an accident on necessity, contradicting the assumption. For the second, the common factor of existence cannot entail the differentiating factor of necessity. For the third, both would be accidents on a third quiddity, again contradictory. For the fourth, existence becomes a separate accident, making the Necessary Being contingent. Thus the claim is proven false.

Key Terms
الوجوب بالذات
الوجود
عارض
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that the Necessary Being is simply existence without any quiddity.
Correction: This lesson shows that if that were true, then the relationship between necessity and existence would have to fit one of these possibilities, but the first two are logically impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the reason the second division is considered invalid?
A) Because necessity entails existence, making existence an accident.
B) Because existence is a common factor that cannot entail a differentiating factor.
C) Because there is a third concept that entails both.
D) Because neither entails the other, leading to contingency.

Key Takeaway
The first two logical possibilities for the relationship between necessity and existence both lead to contradictions, so they cannot be true for the Necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says existence is a common factor between the Necessary Being and contingent things. When you say 'Allah exists,' do you ever subconsciously imagine His existence as being like yours—just more powerful? How does this lesson challenge that imagination, and what does that mean for how you approach Him in worship?
?
The author argues that a common factor cannot necessitate a specific property. Consider your own life: what are some 'common factors' you share with others (humanity, desires, weaknesses) that you mistakenly think make you distinct? How can you remind yourself daily that your distinction comes from Allah alone, not from an inherent quality?
?
If necessity entailed existence, existence would be an accident added to necessity—like a coat put on. Do you ever treat Allah's attributes as if they are added to His essence, making Him seem composed? How might this understanding of divine simplicity (tawhid al-sifat) change the way you trust in His oneness?

Remaining Divisions and Conclusion
The author refutes the third and fourth divisions and concludes the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third and fourth logical possibilities and how their failures complete the refutation of the claim that the Necessary Being is pure existence.

Core Idea
All four logical possibilities lead to contradiction; the claim is logically false.

Having rejected the first two possibilities, the author now examines the remaining two: that a third concept entails both necessity and existence, and that neither entails the other. Both are shown to be impossible. The conclusion is that the claim that the Necessary Being has no reality except pure existence is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the third division
The third division posits a third concept that entails both necessity and existence. This would make both accidents upon that third quiddity, contradicting the assumption that there is no other quiddity.
The author says 'فقد فرضنا أنه ليس كذلك' – we have supposed it is not so.
2
Read the fourth division
The fourth division posits no entailment between necessity and existence. Then existence would be a separate accident, making the Necessary Being contingent, which is false.
The term 'ممكنا لذاته' means contingent by essence.
3
Understand the final conclusion
Since all four possibilities fail, the only conclusion is that the original claim is false. The author declares it 'باطل فاسد'.
The proof is complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يقال : لا هذا يستلزم ذاك ولا ذالك يستلزم هذا ، وليس هناك ماهية ثالثة تستلزمهما معا . فهذه أقسام أربعة لا مزيد عليها .
أما القسم الأول : وهو أن يقال : الوجوب بالذات يستلزم الوجود . فعلى هذا التقدير ، يكون الوجود عارضا للوجوب بالذات . وقد فرضنا ذلك الوجود، غير عارض بشيء من الماهيات . هذا خلف
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : الوجود يستلزم الوجوب بالذات ، فهذا فاسد لأن مسمى الوجود هو القدر المشترك بينه وبين الممكنات ، وما به المشاركة يمتنع أن يستلزم ما به الممايزة ، لأن الأشياء المتساوية في النوع، يمتنع أن تلزمها لوازم مختلفة بالنوع
وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يقال : حصل هناك مفهوم ثالث ، يستلزم هذين القيدين . فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير يكون الوجوب بالذات ويكون الوجود عارضين لتلك الماهية. وقد فرضنا أنه ليس كذلك . هذا خلف
وأما القسم الرابع : وهو أن لا يكون واحدا منهما مستلزما للآخر ، وليس هناك ثالث يستلزمهما ، فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير لا يكون ذلك الوجود مستلزما للوجوب الذاتي ، بل يكون ذلك الوجود عارضا مفارقا لذلك الوجود ، فحينئذ لا يكون الواجب لذاته، واجبا لذاته ، بل ممكنا لذاته ، وذلك باطل . فثبت بهذا البرهان : أن القول بأن واجب الوجود لذاته لا حقيقة له إلا الوجود المجرد . كلام باطل فاسد
It can be said: neither does this entail that, nor does that entail this, and there is no third quiddity that entails both simultaneously. So these are four divisions with no increase.
As for the first division: it is to say: necessity-by-essence (al-wujūb bi al-dhāt) entails existence (al-wujūd). On this assumption, existence becomes an accident upon necessity-by-essence. And we have supposed that existence does not occur as an accident upon any quiddity. This is a contradiction.
As for the second division: it is to say: existence entails necessity-by-essence. This is invalid because the denotation of existence is the common factor between it and contingent things, and that by which there is participation cannot entail that by which there is differentiation, because things equal in species cannot have differing entailments by species.
As for the third division: it is to say: there occurs a third concept that entails these two qualifications. So we say: on this assumption, both necessity-by-essence and existence would be accidents upon that quiddity. And we have supposed it is not so. This is a contradiction.
As for the fourth division: it is that neither entails the other, and there is no third that entails them both. So we say: on this assumption, that existence is not entailing essential necessity; rather that existence is a separate accident upon that existence. Then the Necessary-by-essence would not be necessary-by-essence, but rather contingent-by-essence, and that is false. Thus it is established by this proof that the statement that the Necessary Being by essence has no reality except pure existence is a false, invalid statement.

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhān) refuting the claim that the Necessary Being has no reality except pure existence. The author presents four logical possibilities for the relationship between necessity-by-essence (al-wujūb bi al-dhāt) and existence (al-wujūd): (1) necessity entails existence, (2) existence entails necessity, (3) a third concept entails both, (4) neither entails the other. For the first, existence would be an accident on necessity, contradicting the assumption. For the second, the common factor of existence cannot entail the differentiating factor of necessity. For the third, both would be accidents on a third quiddity, again contradictory. For the fourth, existence becomes a separate accident, making the Necessary Being contingent. ?

?

The Threefold Division of God's Sourcehood
Sanusi divides the nature of God's sourcehood into three possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three broad possibilities for how God can be the source of possible existents and why the first possibility (existence alone) is invalid.

Core Idea
God's sourcehood cannot be due to existence alone, or everything would be a source.

The author begins the fifth argument by setting up a threefold division. Before examining each possibility, he immediately rules out the simplest one: that God's being the source is due to His existence alone. This lesson introduces the division and the swift refutation of the first option.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Note the three options the author enumerates.
The passage begins with 'الحجة الخامة' (the fifth argument).
2
Identify the three divisions
Find where the author lists: existence alone, existence with a negative qualification, existence with a positive qualification. Write them down.
3
Understand the refutation of the first
The author says the first is false because it would make every existent a source for all effects. Consider why that is absurd.
No rational person would claim every being has the same causal power as God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامة : لا شك أنه تعالى مبدءا لوجود الممكنات ، وكونه مبدءا لها : إما أن يكون لنفس كونه وجودا ، أو لنفس كونه وجودا مع قيد سلبي ، أو لنفس كونه وجودا مع قيد وجودي ، والأول باطل . وإلا لوجب أن يكون كل موجود جنس المبدأ الجميع الآثار الصادرة عن واجب الوجود ، وذلك لا يقوله عاقل ، والثاني باطل لوجهين :
الأول : إن على هذا التقدير يصير القيد العدمي ، جزءا من ماهية المبدأ الأول من حيث إنه مبدأ لغيره ، وذلك محال ، لأن العدم لا يكون علة للوجود ، ولا جزءا من علة الوجود
والثاني : وهو أن المقتضى لذلك السبب إن كان هو طبيعة الوجود ، لزم حصوله في كل الموجودات ، وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون كل وجود متساويا بالذات للمبدأ الأول في اقتضاء كل الآثار. وذلك باطل . وإن كان قيدا آخر سلبيا عاد التقسيم الأول فيه .
وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يقال : إن كون ذلك الوجود مبدأ لوجود الممكنات ، ليس لكونه وجودا فقط ، بل لكونه وجودا مع قيد ثبوتي. فنقول : ذلك القيد الثبوتي إما أن يكون معروضا لذلك الوجود أو عارضا له ، أو لا معروضا له ولا عارضا له. فإن كان معروضا لذلك الوجود ، كان ذلك الوجود عارضا لماهية أخرى ، وقد فرضنا أنه ليس كذلك . هذا خلف . وإن كان عارضا لذلك الوجود فكل عارض فهو محتاج، فالمقتضى لذلك العارض إن كان هو الوجود فقط ، أو الوجود بقيد سلبي ، عادت المحالات المذكورة ، وإن كان المقتضي لذلك العارض هو الوجود بقيد عارض آخر ، كان الكلام فيه كما في الأول ، ولزم التسلسل وهو محال . وبتقدير أن لا يكون التسلسل محالا ، لكنا نقول : هل ههنا شيء يستلزم شيئاً أو لم يحصل ذلك ؟ فإن كان الأول كان ذلك الاستلزام إما لنفس الوجود ، أو لنفس الوجود مع قيد سلبي ، أو لنفس الوجود مع قيد ثبوتي ، وإن كان الثاني كان ذلك
The fifth argument: There is no doubt that He, exalted, is the source (mabda') of the existence of possible things. And His being a source for them: either it is due to His being existence itself, or due to His being existence with a negative qualification, or due to His being existence with a positive qualification. The first is invalid, otherwise it would necessitate that every existent is a genus of the source for all the effects emanating from the Necessary Being, and no rational person would say that. The second is invalid for two reasons:
First: On this assumption, the negative qualification becomes part of the quiddity of the First Source insofar as it is a source for other things, and that is impossible, because non-existence cannot be a cause of existence, nor part of the cause of existence.
Second: That if the determinant of that cause is the nature of existence, it would obtain in all existents, and then it would necessitate that every existence is by essence equal to the First Source in the entailment of all effects. That is false. And if it is another negative qualification, the first division recurs in it.
As for the third division: it is to say: that existence being a source for the existence of possible things is not due to its being existence alone, but rather due to its being existence with a positive qualification. So we say: that positive qualification is either a substrate (ma'rūḍ) for that existence or an accident (ʿāriḍ) upon it, or neither a substrate nor an accident. If it is a substrate for that existence, then that existence would be an accident upon another quiddity, and we have supposed that it is not so. This is a contradiction. And if it is an accident upon that existence, then every accident is in need (muḥtāj). So the determinant for that accident: if it is existence alone, or existence with a negative qualification, the aforementioned impossibilities recur. And if the determinant for that accident is existence with another accidental qualification, the discussion regarding it is as in the first, and infinite regress (tasalsul) follows, which is impossible. And on the assumption that infinite regress is not impossible, we say: is there here something that entails something, or not? If the first, then that entailment is either due to existence itself, or due to existence with a negative qualification, or due to existence with a positive qualification. And if the second, then that...
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage is the fifth proof (al-ḥujja al-khāmisah) concerning the nature of God as the source (mabda') of possible existents. The author divides the possibilities into three: (1) God's being a source is due to His existence alone, (2) due to existence with a negative qualification, or (3) due to existence with a positive qualification. The first is rejected because it would imply that every existent is a source, which is absurd. The second is rejected for two reasons: (a) it makes non-existence part of the quiddity of the source, which is impossible since non-existence cannot cause existence, and (b) if the determinant is existence itself, then all existents would be equal to God in producing effects. The third division is examined in detail: the positive qualification must be either a substrate or an accident upon existence. If a substrate, existence becomes an accident on another quiddity, contradicting the assumption. If an accident, then existence depends on the accident, leading to an infinite regress. The proof concludes by showing that even if infinite regress were allowed, the same three possibilities recur regarding entailment. The argument is structured as a series of conditional refutations, each leading to a contradiction or impossibility.

Key Terms
مبدأ
قيد سلبي
قيد وجودي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the first division (existence alone) invalid?
A) It leads to infinite regress.
B) It would make every existent a source of all effects.
C) It requires a negative qualification.
D) It contradicts God's uniqueness.

Key Takeaway
The first possibility is ruled out because it would entail that every existent has the same creative power as God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat God as merely 'existing' in your life, or do you recognize that He must be qualified with specific attributes to be your actual source? This proof shows that existence alone does not make Him your Creator. When you pray, do you acknowledge Him as the One with positive qualifications—the All-Powerful, the All-Knowing—or just a distant 'existence'? Commit today to reflecting on His attributes before your next prayer.
?
The first option is rejected because it would mean every existent is a source. Yet in your daily life, do you ever act as if you are a source—self-sufficient, independent, in control? Where have you forgotten that you are a contingent being, entirely dependent on the uniquely qualified Source? Take a moment to list three ways you have relied on yourself instead of Him, and ask His forgiveness.
?
This argument uses logic to strip away false conceptions of God. Is your understanding of God 'just existence,' or do you actively affirm the extra qualifications that make Him truly the Lord? This is not an academic question—it shapes your worship. If you only see God as 'existence,' how will you fear Him, love Him, or trust Him? Decide right now to learn one of His names that shows His active role as source, and meditate on it today.

Refutation of the Second Division: Negative Qualification
Sanusi refutes the second division with two detailed objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two reasons why the second division (existence with a negative qualification) is invalid.

Core Idea
Non-existence cannot be a cause of existence, nor can it be part of the source's quiddity.

Having ruled out existence alone, the author now tackles the second possibility: that God's sourcehood is due to existence plus a negative qualification. He gives two specific objections. This lesson examines both reasons carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the Arabic and translation for the two reasons. Note the author's concise style.
The author says 'والثاني باطل لوجهين' (the second is invalid for two reasons).
2
First reason: non-existence as part of quiddity
If a negative qualification is added, then non-existence becomes part of the essence of the source. That is impossible because non-existence cannot cause existence.
This is a metaphysical principle: absence cannot produce presence.
3
Second reason: determinant leads to equality
If the determinant of sourcehood is existence itself (the positive part), then all existents would share that determinant and be equal in producing effects. If the determinant is another negative, the same division recurs.
This second reason mirrors the first division's refutation in a way.

Refutation of the Third Division: Positive Qualification and Infinite Regress
Sanusi refutes the third division by examining the nature of the positive qualification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the third division (existence with a positive qualification) leads to infinite regress or contradiction.

Core Idea
Any positive qualification added to existence either makes existence dependent or leads to an endless chain of dependencies.

The third possibility is that God's sourcehood is due to existence plus a positive qualification. The author examines what that qualification could be: it must either be a substrate, an accident, or neither. Each option is shown to be problematic, ultimately leading to infinite regress (tasalsul). This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the third division analysis
Read the long passage starting from 'وأما القسم الثالث'. Note the three sub-options for the positive qualification.
The author uses technical terms: معروض (substrate) and عارض (accident).
2
Follow the three sub-cases
First: if the qualification is a substrate, then existence becomes an accident on something else, contradicting the assumption that God is pure existence. Second: if it is an accident, then existence is dependent, and we need a determinant for that accident, leading to infinite regress. Third: if neither, it is unintelligible.
The regress argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Consider the conditional on infinite regress
The author adds a final conditional: even if infinite regress were allowed, the same three options reappear for entailment, showing the problem is inescapable.
This strengthens the argument by showing that no escape is possible.

?

What in your heart resists accepting that God needs no 'qualities' to be the source of creation? The Positive Condition: Subject or Accident?
Sanusi refutes the positive condition by dividing it into subject or accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the positive condition cannot be a subject or an accident of the First Principle's existence without leading to circularity or infinite regress.

Core Idea
Adding a positive condition to pure existence destroys its self-sufficiency.

The third division claims that the First Principle's existence is the source of possibles only because of a positive condition added to existence. Sanusi examines this condition by asking whether it is a subject or an accident of existence. By analyzing each possibility, he shows that both lead to contradictions, forcing us to reject the claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi presents a dichotomy.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage says the positive condition is either a subject (ma'ruḍ) for existence, an accident ('āriḍ) for it, or neither. Focus on the first two as the third is dismissed.
3
Understand the refutation of the subject case
If the condition is a subject, existence becomes an accident of another quiddity, contradicting the assumption that the First Principle has no quiddity apart from existence. This is a circularity (khulf).
4
Understand the refutation of the accident case
If the condition is an accident, it is contingent and needs a determinant. That determinant cannot be existence alone or with a negative condition (already refuted), and if it is existence with another accident, infinite regress follows.
Both branches of the accident case lead to impossibilities.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
Since both possibilities are impossible, the claim that the First Principle's existence is with a positive condition is false. This is a key step in the overall refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يقال : إن كون ذلك الوجود مبدأ لوجود الممكنات ، ليس لكونه وجودا فقط ، بل لكونه وجودا مع قيد ثبوتي. فنقول : ذلك القيد الثبوتي إما أن يكون معروضا لذلك الوجود أو عارضا له ، أو لا معروضا له ولا عارضا له. فإن كان معروضا لذلك الوجود ، كان ذلك الوجود عارضا لماهية أخرى ، وقد فرضنا أنه ليس كذلك . هذا خلف . وإن كان عارضا لذلك الوجود فكل عارض فهو محتاج، فالمقتضى لذلك العارض إن كان هو الوجود فقط ، أو الوجود بقيد سلبي ، عادت المحالات المذكورة ، وإن كان المقتضي لذلك العارض هو الوجود بقيد عارض آخر ، كان الكلام فيه كما في الأول ، ولزم التسلسل وهو محال . وبتقدير أن لا يكون التسلسل محالا ، لكنا نقول : هل ههنا شيء يستلزم شيئاً أو لم يحصل ذلك ؟ فإن كان الأول كان ذلك الاستلزام إما لنفس الوجود ، أو لنفس الوجود مع قيد سلبي ، أو لنفس الوجود مع قيد ثبوتي ، وإن كان الثاني كان ذلك نفياً للاستلزام ، وحينئذ لا يجب في المبدأ الأول كونه مبدأ لغيره ، ولا يجب في غيره كونه محتاجاً إليه ، بل ربما انقلب فيصير الواجب ممكناً ، والممكن واجباً . وكل ذلك باطل محال . فثبت أن القول بأن حقيقة واجب الوجود وجود مجرد يفضي إلى هذه الأباطيل ، فكان القول به باطلاً .
As for the third division: that it is said: that that existence is the principle of the existence of possibles not because it is existence only, but because it is existence with a positive condition. We say: that positive condition either is a subject (ma'rud) for that existence or an accident (arid) for it, or neither a subject nor an accident. If it is a subject for that existence, then that existence would be an accident for another quiddity, and we have assumed that it is not so — this is a circularity (khulf). If it is an accident for that existence, then every accident is needful, so the determinant of that accident — if it is existence alone or existence with a negative condition — the aforementioned impossibilities return; and if the determinant of that accident is existence with another accident, then the discussion about it is like the first, and an infinite regress (tasalsul) follows, which is impossible. And assuming that infinite regress is not impossible, we would say: is there here something that entails something else or not? If the first, then that entailment is either for existence itself, or for existence itself with a negative condition, or for existence itself with a positive condition; and if the second, then that is a negation of entailment, and then it is not necessary for the First Principle to be a principle for other things, nor necessary for other things to be in need of Him; rather it is possible that the necessary becomes contingent and the contingent necessary. All that is false and impossible. Thus it is established that the statement that the reality of the Necessary Being is pure existence leads to these absurdities, so the statement is false.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the third division in an argument about the nature of the First Principle. The division under consideration is the claim that the Necessary Being's existence is the principle of possibles not because of existence alone, but because of existence with a positive condition (qayd thubuti). The author analyzes this condition: it must either be a subject (ma'rud) for that existence, or an accident (arid) for it, or neither. If it is a subject, then existence becomes an accident for another quiddity, but we have already assumed that the First Principle has no quiddity apart from existence—this is a circularity. If the condition is an accident of existence, then every accident is contingent (needs a determinant). The determinant could be existence alone or with a negative condition—both lead to previously refuted impossibilities. If the determinant is existence with another accident, an infinite regress occurs, which is impossible. Even if regress were permissible, the author examines entailment (istilzam): if there is entailment, it falls into the same three subdivisions; if there is no entailment, then the First Principle would not necessarily be a principle for others, and contingency relations could be reversed—both absurd. Therefore, the claim that the Necessary Being is pure existence is false.

Misconception: A positive condition can be added to pure existence without leading to contradictions.
Correction: The passage shows that a positive condition, whether as subject or accident, leads to circularity or infinite regress, both impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the positive condition is a subject for existence, what follows?
A) The existence becomes an accident of another quiddity.
B) Infinite regress follows.
C) The condition is unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
The positive condition cannot be a subject or accident of the First Principle without contradiction.

?

In your own life, do you seek to base your certainty in God on some additional 'reason' or 'proof' beyond the simple recognition of His existence? How does this lesson call you to a purer form of tawhid, where you accept God's existence as the absolute ground without any added conditions?

Entailment and the Final Refutation
Sanusi uses entailment analysis to complete the refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the entailment analysis demonstrates the absurdity of the claim that the Necessary Being is pure existence.

Core Idea
Even if regress were allowed, the entailment argument collapses possibility into necessity.

Sanusi continues by entertaining the possibility that infinite regress is not impossible. He then examines entailment: whether the First Principle entails something or not. Each branch leads to absurd consequences, including the reversal of necessity and contingency. This seals the refutation of the third division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the hypothetical assumption and the two main branches of entailment.
2
Understand the hypothetical
Sanusi says: even if infinite regress were not impossible (a concession), the argument continues. This shows he is considering all possibilities.
3
Analyze the two branches of entailment
Either there is something that entails something else (istilzam) or there is not. The first branch divides into three: entailment for existence itself, existence with negative condition, or with positive condition. The second branch negates entailment.
4
Recognize the absurdity of no entailment
If there is no entailment, the First Principle may not be a principle for others, and necessity/contingency could be reversed — an impossible consequence.
This is a powerful argument showing the necessity of entailment.
5
Conclude the overall refutation
The final sentence establishes that the claim 'the Necessary Being is pure existence' leads to absurdities and is therefore false. This is the main conclusion of the entire passage.

Existence Cannot Stand Alone: The Need for Quiddity
Sixth proof: existence requires quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why rational intuition requires that existence cannot be conceived without a quiddity (essence).

Core Idea
Existence without a quiddity is rationally inconceivable.

This sixth proof tackles a fundamental assumption: can we think of existence as a bare fact without any essence? The author argues that reason itself rejects this. Drawing on philosophical definitions, he shows that existence is nothing but 'being-in-reality', and that such a notion only makes sense if there is something (a quiddity) that is in reality. This lesson will walk through the proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Try to grasp the overall flow before diving into analysis.
The passage ends abruptly; focus on what is present.
2
Identify the main claim
Find where the author states that existence without quiddity is impossible. Notice the role of 'clear reason' (صريح العقل).
3
Understand the philosophers' definition
The passage cites philosophers who define the existent as 'being-in-reality' itself. See how this definition supports the argument.
4
Reflect on the rational intuition
Consider why the 'intuition of reason' (بديهة العقل) is invoked as final evidence. Why is this considered decisive?

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة وهي الحجة السادسة : إن صريح العقل يشهد بأن فرض شيء لا حقيقة له إلا مجرد الحصول في الأعيان يكون محالا ، بل لا بد وأن يفرض العقل ماهية وحقيقة ، ثم يحكم عليها بأنها حاصلة في الأعيان ، فأما فرض موجود لا ماهية له ، ولا حقيقة له إلا مجرد الحصول في الأعيان ، فهذا لا يقبله العقل البتة والذي يزيد هذا الكلام تقريرا : ذلك لأن الحكماء ذكروا في باب الوجود أن قالوا : الموجود هو نفس الكون في الأعيان ، لا ما به يحصل الكون في الأعيان ، وأطنبوا في تقرير هذا المعنى وفي إيضاحه ، وإذا ثبت أن الوجود ليس له حقيقة إلا مجرد الحصول في الأعيان ، فالحصول في الأعيان يمتنع تقرره في العقل إلا إذا فرض العقل ماهية يحكم عليها بأنها حصلت في الأعيان ، وإذا كان كذلك ، كان هذا اعترافا بأن الوجود بدون الماهية لا يتقرر البتة ، بل بديهة العقل حاكمة بأنه متى حصل معنى الحصول في الأعيان ، فقد حصلت هناك ماهية هي المحكوم عليها بأنها حصلت في الأعيان ، وذلك يفيد الجزم بأن الوجود بدون الماهية لا يعقل البتة ، ومما يقوي ذلك : أن الحكماء اتفقوا على أن طبيعة الوجود لا يمكن تعقلها وحدها ، بل ما لم يفرض العقل أمرا من الأمور يحكم عليه بأنه حصل في الأعيان ، استحال منه إدراك معنى الحصول في الأعيان. فإذا كان معنى الوجود أمرا بلغ في الضعف والحاجة إلى حيث لا
Issue: and it is the sixth proof: Verily, clear reason bears witness that assuming a thing that has no reality except mere being-in-reality (al-husul fi al-a'yan) is impossible; rather, reason must suppose a quiddity (mahiyya) and a reality, then judge of it that it is realized in external reality. As for assuming an existent that has no quiddity and no reality except mere being-in-reality, reason does not accept this at all. And what reinforces this statement is that the philosophers mentioned in the chapter on existence that they said: the existent (al-mawjud) is the very being-in-reality, not that by which being-in-reality is obtained; and they elaborated in establishing this meaning and clarifying it. And if it is established that existence has no reality except mere being-in-reality, then being-in-reality cannot be established in reason unless reason supposes a quiddity upon which it judges that it has been realized in external reality. And if it is so, then this is an admission that existence without quiddity is not established at all; rather, the intuition of reason judges that whenever the meaning of being-in-reality occurs, there has occurred a quiddity that is judged to have been realized in external reality. And this yields certainty that existence without quiddity is not conceivable at all. And what strengthens that is that the philosophers agreed that the nature of existence cannot be conceived by itself; rather, unless reason supposes something among things upon which it judges that it has been realized in external reality, it is impossible for it to perceive the meaning of being-in-reality. So if the meaning of existence is a matter that has reached weakness and need to the point that it does not
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof, introduced as a new issue. The proof argues that existence cannot be conceived without a quiddity. The author begins by stating that clear reason testifies that assuming something with no reality except mere 'being-in-reality' (al-husul fi al-a'yan) is impossible. Rather, reason must first conceive a quiddity (mahiyya) and then judge that it exists in external reality. The claim that there could be an existent with no quiddity except mere being-in-reality is rejected by reason. To reinforce this, the author cites the philosophers (al-hukama') who define 'existents' as being-in-reality itself, not that by which being-in-reality is obtained. Once it is established that existence is nothing but being-in-reality, it follows that being-in-reality cannot be conceived in the mind without the mind positing a quiddity that it judges to be in reality. This means that existence without quiddity is not established at all. The intuition of reason (badiha al-aql) rules that wherever the meaning of being-in-reality occurs, there is a quiddity that is judged to be in reality. This yields certainty that existence without quiddity is inconceivable. The author adds that the philosophers agree that the nature of existence cannot be conceived alone; unless the mind supposes something and judges it to be in reality, it cannot grasp the meaning of being-in-reality. The passage ends with a remark that the meaning of existence is so weak and needy that it cannot stand alone—the text cuts off, but the argument is clear: existence requires quiddity.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence can be conceived as a bare fact without any quiddity.
Correction: Reason cannot conceive of existence without simultaneously conceiving a quiddity that exists; existence is always dependent on a quiddity.

Checkpoint

B) First suppose a quiddity, then judge it to be in reality.
C) Accept that existence is the same as being-in-reality without quiddity.
D) Deny that existence has any meaning.

Key Takeaway
Existence is not a standalone concept; it always requires a quiddity upon which it is predicated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you ever treat it as a mere fact without asking 'what am I?' This lesson says that existence always comes with a quiddity. What is the quiddity that defines who you are? Are you living in accordance with it or ignoring it?
?
If existence is so weak that it cannot be conceived without a quiddity, then everything around you points to a deeper reality. How does this truth affect your awareness of the Creator whose existence is His very essence? Does it humble you that your own existence is dependent on a quiddity given by Him?
?
Think of something you deeply desire—does its existence come before its nature in your mind? This lesson shows that the quiddity must come first. How might this reorder your priorities: instead of chasing existence (success, wealth), focus on the quiddity (purpose, character) that gives that existence meaning?

The Sixth Argument: Existence Without Quiddity is Inconceivable
The author presents the sixth argument that existence without quiddity is rationally impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the intellect cannot conceive existence without a quiddity and how this argument refutes the conception of the Necessary Being as pure existence.

Core Idea
Existence without quiddity is rationally inconceivable; the mind always requires a subject.

In this sixth proof, the author presents a rational argument against the idea that the Necessary Being is pure existence without any quiddity. He demonstrates that the intellect always needs to posit a quiddity that exists, making bare existence unintelligible. The argument draws on philosophical discussions about the nature of existence and exposes a paradox in the opposing view.

Guided Path
1
Understand the starting premise
Read the author's claim that the intellect cannot accept a thing whose only reality is its mere obtaining in external existence. Note that he insists the mind must always suppose a quiddity and then judge that it exists.
This is the foundational intuition.
2
Examine the philosophical distinction
The author cites philosophers who say 'the existent is the very being in external existence, not the means by which being occurs.' Grasp the distinction between existence as the act of being and the quiddity that receives it.
3
Trace the implication
If existence is only obtaining in reality, then to conceive it, the intellect must conceive a quiddity that obtains. This shows existence without quiddity is unintelligible.
4
Consider the paradox
The author points out that if existence is a single nature in both the Necessary and possible, yet it is independent in the Necessary and dependent on quiddity in the possible, this is incoherent.
This exposes a flaw in the opposing view.
5
Conclude with the absurdity
The author concludes that any fair person will see the view that the Necessary Being is pure existence is evidently false to sound intellect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة ، وهي الحجة السادسة : إن صريح العقل يشهد بأن فرض شيء لا حقيقة له إلا مجرد الحصول في الأعيان يكون محالا ، بل لا بد وأن يفرض العقل ماهية وحقيقة ، ثم يحكم عليها بأنها حاصلة في الأعيان ، فأما فرض موجود لا ماهية له ، ولا حقيقة له إلا مجرد الحصول في الأعيان ، فهذا لا يقبله العقل البتة والذي يزيد هذا الكلام تقريرا : ذلك لأن الحكماء ذكروا في باب الوجود أن قالوا : الموجود هو نفس الكون في الأعيان ، لا ما به يحصل الكون في الأعيان ، وأطنبوا في تقرير هذا المعنى وفي إيضاحه ، وإذا ثبت أن الوجود ليس له حقيقة إلا مجرد الحصول في الأعيان ، فالحصول في الأعيان [يمتنع] تقرره في العقل إلا إذا فرض العقل ماهية يحكم عليها بأنها حصلت في الأعيان ، وإذا كان كذلك ، كان هذا اعترافا بأن الوجود بدون الماهية لا يتقرر البتة ، بل بديهة العقل حاكمة بأنه متى حصل معنى الحصول في الأعيان ، فقد حصلت هناك ماهية هي المحكوم عليها بأنها حصلت في الأعيان ، وذلك يفيد الجزم بأن الوجود بدون الماهية لا يعقل البتة ، ومما يقوي ذلك : أن الحكماء اتفقوا على أن طبيعة الوجود لا يمكن تعقلها وحدها ، بل ما لم يفرض العقل أمرا من الأمور يحكم عليه بأنه حصل في الأعيان ، استحال منه إدراك معنى الحصول في الأعيان فإذا كان معنى الوجود أمرا بلغ في الضعف والحاجة إلى حيث لا يمكن تعقله وحده بدون الماهية ، ثم ثبت أن طبيعة الوجود من حيث إنه هو وجود أمر واحد ، في الواجب والممكن ، فكيف تعقل طبيعة الموجود من حيث إنها وجود ، طبيعة واحدة في الواجب وفي الممكن ؟ ثم إنه بلغ في القوة في جانب الواجب إلى حيث كان غنيا عن كل سواه ، وكان كل ما سواه محتاجا إليه ، وبلغ في الضعف والحاجة في جانب الممكن إلى حيث كان محتاجا إلى الماهية في الخارج وفي الذهن ، حتى إن العقل لا يمكنه أن يتصوره وحده ، بل ما لم يجعله تابعا لغيره ، فإنه لا يمكنه إدراكه وتصوره. وكل من أنصف علم أن هذا الكلام معلوم البطلان ببديهة العقل .
The sixth proof: Verily sound intellect bears witness that assuming a thing that has no reality except mere obtaining in external existence is impossible. Rather, the intellect must suppose a quiddity and reality, then judge that it is obtained in external existence. As for assuming an existent with no quiddity and no reality except mere obtaining in external existence, the intellect does not accept that at all. What strengthens this speech in confirmation is that the philosophers mentioned in the chapter on existence that they said: 'The existent is the very being in external existence, not the means by which being in external existence occurs.' And they elaborated in confirming this meaning and clarifying it. And if it is established that existence has no reality except mere obtaining in external existence, then obtaining in external existence cannot be established in the intellect unless the intellect supposes a quiddity that it judges to have obtained in external existence. And if that is so, this is an admission that existence without quiddity is not established at all. Rather, the intuition of the intellect rules that whenever the meaning of obtaining in external existence occurs, there has occurred a quiddity that is the subject of the judgment that it obtained in external existence. And that yields certainty that existence without quiddity is not intelligible at all. And among what strengthens that is that the philosophers agreed that the nature of existence cannot be conceived alone; rather, unless the intellect supposes something that it judges to have obtained in external existence, it is impossible for it to perceive the meaning of obtaining in external existence. So if the meaning of existence is a thing that has reached such weakness and need that it cannot be conceived alone without quiddity, and then it is established that the nature of existence inasmuch as it is existence is one thing in the Necessary and the possible, then how can one conceive the nature of the existent inasmuch as it is existence as a single nature in the Necessary and in the possible? Then it has reached such power in the aspect of the Necessary that it is independent of everything else, and everything else is in need of it, and it has reached such weakness and need in the aspect of the possible that it is in need of quiddity in the external and in the mind, such that the intellect cannot conceive it alone; rather, unless it makes it dependent on something else, it cannot perceive and conceive it. And everyone who is fair knows that this speech is known to be false by the intuition of the intellect.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the sixth argument) demonstrating the impossibility of conceiving existence without a quiddity. The author begins by stating the starting premise: the intellect cannot accept a thing whose only reality is its external existence; it must always posit a quiddity that is appraised as existing. He then strengthens this by citing the philosophers' view that existence is precisely the being-in-external-reality, not the means of being-in-reality. From this, he argues that since existence is only that, its conception in the intellect inevitably requires a quiddity to be the subject of the judgment 'obtained in reality.' The author then reinforces the argument by noting that philosophers agree the nature of existence cannot be understood alone; one must always conceive something else (a quiddity) that exists. He then points out the paradox: if existence is a single nature in both the Necessary Being and the possible, yet it is so weak in the possible that it cannot be conceived without quiddity while it is independent in the Necessary, this is incoherent. The conclusion is that the view that the Necessary Being's essence is pure existence without quiddity is evidently false to any fair-minded person.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think existence can be abstracted from quiddity as a separate entity.
Correction: The argument shows that they are inseparable in conception; the intellect cannot conceive bare existence without a quiddity.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth argument, what must the intellect always posit when conceiving existence?
A) A quiddity that is judged to be existent
B) The essence of the Necessary Being
C) The negation of quiddity
D) The attributes of existence

Key Takeaway
The intellect necessarily requires a quiddity to conceive existence, so the idea of the Necessary Being as pure existence without quiddity is rationally untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah exists,' do you ever stop to think what you are actually affirming? Is your mind merely uttering a word, or do you truly conceive a Being whose existence is inseparable from His attributes? This lesson claims that to even think of existence, you must think of a subject. Have you ever tried to think of Allah without any attribute—just 'existence'? Does that not feel like an empty abstraction? How then can you ensure that your worship is directed to the Real Being, not a mental construct?
?
The philosophers say existence cannot be understood alone; it needs a quiddity. But the Necessary Being is not like other things. If you cannot conceive of existence without a quiddity, how do you conceive of Allah? Does your mind create an image or a definition? This lesson warns against assuming that Allah's essence is pure existence without quiddity. How does that challenge your conception of God, and what step will you take today to purify your understanding according to the revelation?
?
The passage concludes by saying anyone who is fair knows the falsehood of the opposing view. Are you fair? Do you submit your intellect to the truth even when it challenges comfortable assumptions? This argument may be abstract, but it guards against a dangerous misconception about Allah. Have you ever unknowingly harbored such a misconception? What will you do to correct it?

The Seventh Argument: Existence vs. Essence
Proof that divine essence is not identical to existence via self-evidence of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh argument that the divine essence is not identical to existence, based on the contrast between the self-evidence of existence and the unknowability of the essence.

Core Idea
Existence is self-evident; God's essence is unknown; therefore they are distinct.

This lesson presents the seventh argument from the philosophers: a simple but powerful proof that God's reality is not the same as existence. The argument relies on two agreed-upon points: that existence is intuitively clear, and that the specific reality of God is hidden from human understanding. By comparing these two, the author shows that the divine essence must be other than existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the argument's structure emerge.
Pay attention to the two premises and the conclusion.
2
Identify the first premise
The first premise: existence is an evident, self-evident conception (taṣawwur badīhī). This means it requires no definition.
The philosophers agree on this.
3
Identify the second premise
The second premise: the specific reality (al-ḥaqīqa al-mukhāṣṣa) of the Necessary Being is unknown to humans. They express this as 'the inner reality of God's essence is unknown.'
This is also agreed upon.
4
Follow the logical conclusion
Since existence is primary in understanding ('ūlī bi-l-taṣdīq) and the essence is not even conceivable, it follows decisively that the reality of God is other than His existence.
The argument uses a contrast in epistemic priority.
5
Review key terms and misunderstanding
Note the terms: taṣawwur, badīhī, ḥaqīqa mukhāṣṣa, kunnah. Also consider the common misunderstanding about tasawwur vs tasdiq.
The argument works even if we read 'ūlī bi-l-taṣawwur' instead of 'ūlī bi-l-taṣdīq'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : اتفق الحكماء على أن تصور الوجود تصور بديهي جلي ، غني عن كل التعريفات ، واتفقوا أيضا على أن الحقيقة المخصوصة لواجب الوجود غير معلومة للبشر وعبروا عن هذا المعنى بهذه العبارة وهي قولهم : كنه ذات الله تعالى غير معلوم للبشر ، ولما كان الوجود أولي بالتصديق وكانت الحقيقة المخصوصة غير متصورة ، فضلا عن أن تكون أولية التصور ، كان هذا برهانا قاطعا في أن حقيقة الله تعالى غير وجوده .
The seventh proof: The philosophers agreed that the conception of existence is an evident, self-evident conception, free of all definitions, and they also agreed that the specific reality of the Necessary Being is unknown to humans, and they expressed this meaning with this expression: 'The inner reality of the essence of God Almighty is unknown to humans.' And since existence is prior in assent, and the specific reality is unconceived, let alone being prior in conception, this was a decisive proof that the reality of God Almighty is other than His existence.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the seventh argument) based on the contrast between the self-evidence of existence and the unknowability of the divine essence. The author first establishes two agreed-upon premises from the philosophers: (1) the conception of existence is intuitively clear and needs no definition, and (2) the specific reality (al-ḥaqīqa al-mukhāṣṣa) of the Necessary Being is unknown to humans—they explicitly say that the inner reality (kunnah) of God's essence is unknown. Then the author draws the conclusion: since existence is primary in understanding (whether in assent or conception, depending on the reading), but the specific reality of God is not even conceivable, let alone primary, it follows that the divine essence is not identical to existence. The argument is a decisive proof (burhān qāṭiʿ) that God's reality is other than His existence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument conflates 'conception' (taṣawwur) with 'assent' (taṣdīq), but the argument works for either.
Correction: The argument holds even if we read 'ūlī bi-l-taṣawwur' (prior in conception) instead of 'ūlī bi-l-taṣdīq' (prior in assent), because the essence is neither conceived nor assented to.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The self-evidence of existence and the unknowability of the divine essence prove that existence and essence are distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The sages admit they cannot know God's essence. Have you ever claimed to know what God 'is' or 'must be' in a way that goes beyond what He has revealed? Confess one area where you need to surrender your assumptions and trust His revelation instead.
?
You rely on your own existence every moment, yet the lesson says existence is distinct from the divine reality. Are you treating your own life as an end in itself or as a pointer to the One whose essence is unknown? What concrete step can you take today to shift your focus from your own existence to the reality of God?

The Seventh Proof: Essence and Existence Distinguished
The author presents the seventh proof, based on two agreed premises, followed by an objection and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the self-evident nature of existence and the unknowability of God's essence prove that essence and existence are distinct in God.

Core Idea
Existence is self-evident; God's essence is not — they must be distinct.

This lesson presents the seventh proof for the distinction between God's essence and His existence. Drawing on two key agreements among philosophers — that existence is self-evident and that the specific reality of God is unknowable — the author constructs a decisive argument. We will trace the reasoning step by step, and also address an important objection that could confuse the matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the complete passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall argument. Notice the structure: premises, conclusion, objection, response.
The passage includes an inserted objection from 'the caller to God'.
2
Identify the two premises
The proof relies on two premises agreed upon by philosophers: existence is self-evident, and the specific reality of the Necessary Being is unknown. Make sure you see these clearly.
The term 'self-evident' means it does not need definition or proof.
3
Understand the conclusion
From the premises, conclude that existence and essence are distinct because existence is primary in conception while essence is not even conceived.
If they were identical, the essence would be as self-evident as existence.
4
Examine the objection and response
The author raises an objection: maybe the unknowability refers to attributes, not essence. Follow the three reasons why this interpretation is rejected.
The response distinguishes between essence and attributes and notes that attributes (negative and relational) are known.
5
Reinforce the premises
Even if the premises are not conceded, they are proven elsewhere. This shows the proof is solid regardless of agreement.
This strengthens the argument by referring to other established proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : اتفق الحكماء على أن تصور الوجود تصور بديهي جلي ، غني عن كل التعريفات ، واتفقوا أيضا على أن الحقيقة المخصوصة لواجب الوجود غير معلومة للبشر وعبروا عن هذا المعنى بهذه العبارة وهي قولهم : كنه ذات الله تعالى غير معلوم للبشر، ولما كان الوجود أولي بالتصديق، وكانت الحقيقة المخصوصة غير متصورة ، فضلا عن أن تكون أولية التصور ، كان هذا برهانا قاطعا في أن حقيقة الله تعالى ، غير وجوده . وقال الداعي إلى الله رحمه الله حقا. إني لكثير التعجب من هؤلاء الأفاضل كيف جمعوا بين هذين المذهبين ؟ فإن قيل : مرادهم بقولهم : إن كنه حقيقة الله تعالى غير معلوم ومتصور للبشر : هو أن البشر ما أحاط عقلهم بكنه صفات الله تعالى ؟ قلنا : هذا بعيد جدا ، لأن معرفة الصفات غير ، ومعرفة الذات غير. والقوم قالوا : كنه الذات غير معلوم ، فكيف يجوز صرف هذا الكلام إلى الصفات ؟ وأيضا : فالقوم حصروا صفات الله تعالى في : السلوب وفي الإضافات، وهذان القسمان معلومان ، فكيف يقال : المراد أن الصفات غير معلومة ؟ وأيضا : فهب أن القوم لم يسلموا بأن الوجود بديهي التصور ، ولم يسلموا أن كنه ذات الله تعالى غير معلوم للبشر ، لكن هاتان المقدمتان قد صحتا بالبراهين اليقينية. أما الأول ففي كتاب الوجود . وأما الثاني ففي المسألة المشتملة على أن كنه حقيقة الله تعالى غير معلوم للبشر ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فقد حصل المطلوب على أظهر الوجوه .
The seventh proof: The philosophers agreed that the conception of existence is a clear self-evident conception, independent of all definitions, and they also agreed that the specific reality of the Necessary Being is unknown to humans, and they expressed this meaning with this statement: 'The intrinsic reality (kunnh) of the essence of God Most High is unknown to humans.' And since existence is more primary in assent, and the specific reality is not conceived, let alone being primary in conception, this is a decisive proof that the reality of God Most High is other than His existence. And the caller to God said—may God have mercy on him—'Indeed, I greatly wonder at these virtuous ones how they combine these two positions?' For if it is said: Their intention in saying that the intrinsic reality of the essence of God Most High is unknown and unconceived by humans is that humans' intellects have not encompassed the intrinsic reality of the attributes of God Most High? We say: This is very far, because knowledge of the attributes is one thing, and knowledge of the essence is another. And the people said: the intrinsic reality of the essence is unknown, so how is it permissible to divert this speech to the attributes? And also: The people restricted the attributes of God Most High to the negative attributes (salub) and relational attributes (idafat), and these two categories are known. So how can it be said that what is meant is that the attributes are unknown? And also: Suppose that the people did not concede that existence is self-evident in conception, and did not concede that the intrinsic reality of the essence of God Most High is unknown to humans, but these two premises have been verified by certain proofs. As for the first, it is in the book on existence. As for the second, it is in the problem which includes that the intrinsic reality of the essence of God Most High is unknown to humans. And if the matter is such, then the desired result has been obtained in the clearest manner.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof that the essence of God is distinct from His existence. The author begins by citing agreement among philosophers on two points: first, that the conception of existence is self-evident and does not require definition; second, that the specific reality of the Necessary Being is unknowable to humans. They express the latter by saying 'the intrinsic reality of the essence of God Most High is unknown to humans.' From these two premises, the author draws a conclusion: since existence is more primary in conceptualization and affirmation than the specific reality (which is not even conceived, let alone being primary), it follows that the reality of God is other than His existence. If they were identical, then the reality of God would be as self-evident as existence, but it is not. The author then inserts an objection in the voice of 'the caller to God' expressing wonder at how some scholars combine the two positions (perhaps that existence is self-evident and that the essence is unknowable, yet still claim that essence and existence are identical). The objection is posed: could the statement 'the intrinsic reality of the essence is unknown' actually refer to the attributes of God, not the essence? The author responds firmly: this interpretation is very far-fetched because knowledge of the attributes is distinct from knowledge of the essence. The philosophers themselves said 'the intrinsic reality of the essence', not 'the attributes'. Moreover, they categorized divine attributes into negative attributes (salub) and relational attributes (idafat), both of which are knowable. So if they meant attributes were unknown, they would contradict their own classification. Finally, the author concedes that even if someone does not accept the two premises as agreed upon, they are still established by certain proofs elsewhere. The first premise (existence is self-evident) is proven in the book on existence, and the second premise (the essence is unknowable) is proven in the problem concerning the unknowability of God's essence. With these established, the desired conclusion—that essence and existence are distinct—follows clearly.
