Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The statement 'the intrinsic reality of God's essence is unknown' actually refers to the attributes of God, not the essence itself.
Correction: The author argues that knowledge of attributes is distinct from knowledge of essence, and that the philosophers restricted attributes to negative and relational ones, both of which are knowable. Therefore, the statement cannot be about attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the seventh proof, why must God's essence be distinct from His existence?
A) Because existence is self-evident while God's essence is unknowable
B) Because the philosophers said so
C) Because attributes are unknown
D) Because existence is not a predicate

Key Takeaway
The seventh proof demonstrates that God's essence is distinct from His existence because existence is self-evident while the essence is not, a conclusion reinforced by the refutation of a key objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence is self-evident to your mind, why is God's presence not equally evident to your heart? What in your life has obscured this clear sign? Resolve today to spend five minutes in silent contemplation of the sheer fact that you exist, and let it lead you to the One who gives existence.
?
The passage declares that the essence of God is utterly unknown to you. Does this truth humble the ways you speak of God in casual conversation or define Him in your thoughts? Where have you reduced the Infinite to a concept you can manage? Commit to removing from your du'a any phrase that assumes you can fully grasp His nature.
?
The philosophers limited knowledge of God to negative and relational attributes. When you describe God to yourself or others, do your words reflect the awe of the Unknowable Essence, or do they merely project your own preferences? Before your next prayer, consciously affirm that His reality is beyond your comprehension, and then pray with that humility.

The Eighth Proof: Modality and Existence as Predicate
The author uses logical analysis to show existence is a predicate distinct from essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how logical analysis of propositions reveals that existence is a predicate distinct from the essence of the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
Existence is a predicate, not the subject itself.

This passage presents the eighth proof, which uses the logical components of a proposition to show that existence is attributed to an essence. By analyzing the statement 'God is necessary of existence,' the author demonstrates that the subject of existence is a distinct reality, not existence itself. This lesson will guide you through the logical steps and their theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Review the logical components of a proposition
Read the first part of the passage where the author outlines the four components of a proposition: subject, predicate, relation, and modality. Understand that every proposition has these elements.
The modalities are necessity, possibility, and impossibility.
2
Apply the analysis to 'God is necessary of existence'
Identify the subject (God), predicate (existent), relation (is), and modality (necessity) in the example. Notice that the relation is implied, not stated.
3
Examine the two possibilities for the subject of existence
The author asks: what is the thing that is judged to be existent? It is either existence itself or a reality other than existence. Understand why the first option leads to a tautology.
The first option would produce 'existence must be existent,' which is meaningless.
4
Conclude that existence is a predicate
Since the second option is true, existence is a predicate attached to a distinct quiddity. This shows that in the Necessary Being, existence is not identical to the essence.
This conclusion supports the distinction between essence and existence.

الحجة الثامنة : قد ثبت في المنطق أن الوجوب والإمكان والامتناع جهات . وفسر أهل المنطق هذا المعنى بأن قالوا : كل قضية فلها موضوع ومحمول ، ولا بد وأن يحصل بين الموضوع وبين المحمول نسبة مخصوصة ، وتلك النسبة معناها كون ذلك الموضوع موصوفا بذلك المحمول . وكل قضية فلا بد فيها من حصول هذه الثلاثة . ثم إن موصوفية الموضوع بالمحمول تارة تكون على نعت الوجوب ، وتارة على نعت الإمكان ، وتارة على نعت الامتناع وهذا الكلام يخصونه في المنطق وهو كلام حق معلوم تشهد فطرة العقل بصحته. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إذا قلنا : الله واجب الوجود . فهذه قضية لا بد فيها من موضوع ، ومن محمول ، ومن رابطة ، ومن جهة . أما الموضوع فهو قولنا : الله . أما المحمول فهو قولنا : موجود . وأما الرابطة فمحذوفة في اللفظ ولكنها معتبرة في المعنى ، والتقدير : الله هو موجود . وأما الجهة فهي الوجوب . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : المحكوم عليه بأنه موجود ، إما أن يكون هو كونه موجودا وإما أن يكون حقيقة غير الوجود ، وتلك الحقيقة هي الموضوع للوجود ويكون الوجود محمولا على تلك الحقيقة، والأول باطل ، وإلا لصار تقدير هذه القضية : الوجود يجب أن يكون محكوما عليه بكونه موجودا . ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل . والثاني حق إلا أنه تصريح بأن الوجود محمول ، وأن موضوع هذا المحمول ماهية مخصوصة ، وحقيقة مخصوصة هو المحكوم عليها لهذا
The eighth proof: It is established in logic that necessity (wujub), possibility (imkan), and impossibility (imtina') are modalities (jihaat). And the logicians explained this meaning by saying: Every proposition (qadiyya) has a subject (mawdu') and a predicate (mahmul), and there must occur between the subject and the predicate a specific relation (nisba), and that relation signifies that the subject is described by that predicate. Every proposition inevitably possesses these three. Then the description of the subject by the predicate is sometimes in the mode of necessity, sometimes in the mode of possibility, and sometimes in the mode of impossibility. And they specifically mention this in logic, and it is a true and known statement which the innate disposition of the intellect testifies to its correctness. Now if this is established, we say: When we say 'God is necessary of existence', this is a proposition which inevitably has a subject, a predicate, a relation, and a modality. As for the subject, it is our saying 'God'. As for the predicate, it is our saying 'existent'. As for the relation, it is omitted in wording but considered in meaning, and the implicit meaning is 'God is existent'. As for the modality, it is necessity. Once you know this, we say: The thing that is judged to be existent is either its being existent itself, or it is a reality other than existence, and that reality is the subject for existence, and existence is predicated upon that reality. The first is false; otherwise the estimation of this proposition would become 'Existence must be judged as being existent', and it is known that this is false. The second is true, except that it is a declaration that existence is a predicate, and that the subject of this predicate is a specific quiddity (mahiyya) and a specific reality which is the subject of this predicate.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof, which uses logical analysis of propositions to demonstrate that existence is a predicate attached to a subject essence, implying their distinction. The author begins by reminding us of the logical concept of modalities (jihaat): necessity, possibility, and impossibility. Every categorical proposition consists of a subject (mawdu'), a predicate (mahmul), a relation (nisba) connecting them (in Arabic often omitted but understood), and a modality (jiha) indicating how the predicate applies to the subject (necessarily, possibly, or impossibly). This analysis is well-established in logic and self-evident to the rational mind. The author then applies this to the statement 'God is necessary of existence'. This proposition has: subject = 'God', predicate = 'existent', relation = 'is' (omitted but implied: 'God is existent'), modality = necessity. Now, the author asks: what is the thing that is judged to be existent? It is either the very concept of existence itself, or it is a reality other than existence. The first option is false, because that would yield a proposition like 'existence must be existent', which is a meaningless tautology (the subject and predicate would coincide). The second option is correct: existence is a predicate attributed to a subject that is a specific quiddity (mahiyya) and reality. This shows that in the proposition, existence is a predicate, and its subject is a distinct essence. Therefore, in the Necessary Being, existence is not identical to the essence but is a predicate affirming that the essence exists necessarily. Hence, essence and existence are distinct.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that existence is the very essence of God.
Correction: This proof shows via propositional logic that existence is a predicate, and the subject (essence) is distinct.

Checkpoint

A) Subject, predicate, relation, modality
B) Substance, accident, cause, effect
C) Essence, existence, necessity, possibility
D) Premise, conclusion, inference, syllogism

Key Takeaway
In the proposition 'God is necessary of existence,' existence is a predicate, showing that essence and existence are distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your essence and existence are distinct, then your existence is a gift from the Necessary Being. Are you living as if your existence is borrowed, or as if you are self-sufficient? What would change if you truly internalized that you exist only because of Him?
?
The proof shows that in 'God is necessary of existence', necessity is a modality of the predication, not a property of the predicate itself. How does this reshape your reliance on God—do you act as if His necessity makes Him automatically present, or do you recognize that His necessity demands your total dependence every moment?
?
This logical analysis might feel abstract, but the reality is that every breath you take is a proof of your contingency. Does your heart feel the weight of that, or do you treat existence as an automatic given? What would it look like to live today with the constant awareness that your existence is a predicate—a gift from the One whose existence is necessary?

Ninth Proof: Essence Distinct from Existence in the Necessary Existent
Razi extends the conceivability argument to the Necessary Existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi extends the philosophers' conceivability argument from contingent things to the Necessary Existent, establishing that existence is additional to essence even for God.

Core Idea
The same doubt about existence applies to the Necessary Existent as to contingent things.

This lesson presents a powerful extension of a familiar argument. The philosophers had argued that for contingent things, we can conceive the essence while doubting existence, showing that existence is additional. Razi applies the same test to the Necessary Existent: even after conceiving God as self-sufficient, doubt about His actual existence remains, proving a real distinction between His essence and existence. He then addresses a key objection and turns it back on the objector.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage slowly, noting the structure: presentation of the philosophers' argument, application to the Necessary Existent, objection, and refutation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the core premise
Notice that the argument hinges on the epistemological test: can we conceive X while doubting its existence? If yes, existence is additional.
This test is common in kalam.
3
Follow the objection
The objector claims that for the Necessary Existent, we cannot conceive it without existence. Why? Because it is impossible for it not to exist.
This is the key counterargument.
4
See Razi's refutation
Razi argues that if that were true, everyone who conceives of the Necessary Existent would be certain of its existence, which is false. Therefore the test holds.
This refutation is decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة: إن الحكماء استدلوا على أن وجود الممكنات زائد على ماهياتها. فإن قالوا: يمكننا أن نتصور الماهية مع الشك في الوجود، والمعلوم مغاير لما هو غير معلوم، فالماهية غير الوجود، فنقول: هذا البرهان بعينه قائم في وجود واجب الوجود، وذلك لأنا إذا تصورنا من واجب الوجود، أنه الذي يستغني في وجوده عن الغير، وأنه الذي يستحق الوجود من ذاته. فمن المعلوم بالضرورة أن هذا القدر من التصور لا يوجب الجزم بوجود هذا الموجود، بل بعد حصول هذا التصور يبقى العقل شاكا في أن هذه الحقيقة، هل هي موجودة أم لا؟ فثبت أن في هذا الموضع يمكن تصور الماهية مع الشك في الموجود، وهذا يقتضي كون الماهية مغايرة للوجود، فإن لم يلزم من هذا المعنى حصول التغاير في هذا الموضع، لم يلزم أيضا حصول التغاير في الممكنات. فإن قالوا: الفرق أن ممكن الوجود يمكن انفكاكه عن الوجود، فلا جرم أمكن تعقله مع الشك في الوجود. أما واجب الوجود فإنه لا يمكن تعقله من حيث إنه واجب الوجود إلا مع تعقل الوجود، لأنه من المستحيل أن يقال: إنه مع كونه واجب الوجود، لا يكون موجودا. فنقول: فعلى ما ذكرتم يلزم في كل من تصور أن واجب الوجود لذاته، ما هو؟ أن يجزم بأن واجب الوجود موجود، ولما كان هذا باطلا فاسدا في بديهة العقل، علمنا أنه يمكن حصول تصور واجب الوجود مع الشك في وجوده. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك وجب أن يكون وجوده زائدا على ماهيته.
The ninth proof: The philosophers argued that the existence of contingent things is additional to their quiddities. For they said: We can conceive the quiddity while doubting existence, and what is known is different from what is not known, so the quiddity is other than existence. We say: This very proof holds for the existence of the Necessary Existent. Because if we conceive of the Necessary Existent as one who is independent of others for His existence and who deserves existence from His essence, then it is necessarily known that this amount of conception does not necessitate certainty of the existence of this existent; rather after this conception, the intellect remains doubting whether this reality exists or not. So it is established that in this case, the quiddity can be conceived while doubting existence, and this requires that the quiddity be distinct from existence. For if this meaning does not entail distinction in this case, then it would not entail distinction in contingent things either. If they say: The difference is that a contingent existent can be separate from existence, so it is possible to conceive it while doubting existence. As for the Necessary Existent, it cannot be conceived in as much as it is necessary except with the conception of existence, because it is impossible to say that while being necessary existent, it is not existent. We say: According to what you have mentioned, it would be necessary for everyone who conceives what the Necessary Existent is in itself to be certain that the Necessary Existent exists. And since this is false and corrupt in the natural intuition of the intellect, we know that it is possible to have a conception of the Necessary Existent while doubting its existence. And when the matter is so, it is necessary that His existence be additional to His quiddity.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is the ninth proof that God's existence is additional to His essence. The author begins by presenting the philosophers' argument for contingent things: we can conceive the quiddity (essence) while doubting existence, so existence must be additional to the quiddity. He then applies the same reasoning to the Necessary Existent (wajib al-wujud). He points out that when we conceive of the Necessary Existent as that which is self-sufficient and deserves existence from itself, we still do not necessarily know whether such a being actually exists; doubt remains. Hence, the quiddity of the Necessary Existent can be conceived without affirming existence, proving a distinction. The author then anticipates an objection: contingent things can be separated from existence, but the Necessary Existent cannot be conceived without existence because it is impossible for it not to exist. He refutes by saying that if that were the case, then anyone who conceives of the Necessary Existent would have to be certain of its existence, which is false since many people conceive of it while doubting. Therefore, the objection fails and the conclusion stands: existence is additional to the quiddity of the Necessary Existent.

Correction: Razi does not assume contingency; he merely applies the same epistemological test (conceivability with doubt) to the necessary being.

Checkpoint

B) Conception of the quiddity of the Necessary Existent does not necessitate certainty of its existence.

D) The Necessary Existent's existence is self-evident.

Key Takeaway
The ninth proof shows that even for the Necessary Existent, conception does not necessitate belief in existence, proving that existence is additional to essence.

The Ninth Proof: Existence Added to Essence
Razi presents the ninth proof that God's existence is additional to His essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Fakhr al-Din al-Razi applies the philosophers' argument for contingent beings to prove that God's existence is additional to His essence.

Core Idea
Conceptual separation of essence and existence applies even to God.

Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a proof based on the ability to conceive God's essence while doubting His existence. This mirrors an argument used for contingent beings, showing a key metaphysical distinction. In this lesson, we will follow the logical steps of the proof, including the objection and reply.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the full English translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice how Razi starts by recalling an argument for contingents.
2
Identify the core analogy
Razi applies the same premise—conceiving essence while doubting existence—to God. See how he argues that this shows essence and existence are distinct.
The key phrase: 'this same proof is valid for the Necessary Existent.'
3
Understand the objection and reply
An opponent claims that for God, conceiving Him as Necessary Existent includes existence. Razi replies that if that were true, everyone recognizing God's necessity would be certain of His existence, which is false.
This refutation supports the proof that existence is additional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : إن الحكماء استدلوا على أن وجود الممكنات زائد على ماهياتها . فإن قالوا : يمكننا أن نتصور الماهية مع الشك في الوجود ، والمعلوم مغاير لما هو غير معلوم ، فالماهية غير الوجود ، فنقول : هذا البرهان بعينه قائم في وجود واجب الوجود ، وذلك لأنا إذا تصورنا من واجب الوجود ، أنه الذي يستغني في وجوده عن الغير، وأنه الذي يستحق الوجود من ذاته . فمن المعلوم بالضرورة أن هذا القدر من التصور لا يوجب الجزم بوجود هذا الموجود ، بل بعد حصول هذا التصور يبقى العقل شاكا في أن هذه الحقيقة ، هل هي موجودة أم لا ؟ فثبت أن في هذا الموضع يمكن تصور الماهية مع الشك في الموجود ، وهذا يقتضي كون الماهية مغايرة للوجود ، فإن لم يلزم من هذا المعنى حصول التغاير في هذا الموضع ، لم يلزم أيضا حصول التغاير في الممكنات . فإن قالوا : الفرق أن ممكن الوجود يمكن انفكاكه عن الوجود ، فلا الشك في الوجود . أما واجب الوجود فإنه لا يمكن تعقله من حيث إنه واجب الوجود إلا مع تعقل الوجود ، لأنه من المستحيل أن يقال : إنه مع كونه واجب الوجود ، لا يكون موجودا فنقول : فعلى ما ذكرتم يلزم في كل من تصور أن واجب الوجود لذاته ، ما هو ؟ أن يجزم بأن واجب الوجود موجود ، ولما كان هذا باطلا فاسدا في بديهة العقل ، علمنا أنه يمكن حصول تصور واجب الوجود مع الشك في وجوده . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك وجب أن يكون وجوده زائدا على ماهيته
The ninth proof: The philosophers argued that the existence of contingent beings is additional to their essences. For they said: We can conceive the essence while doubting existence, and what is known is distinct from what is not known, so the essence is other than existence. We say: This same proof is valid for the existence of the Necessary Existent. Because if we conceive of the Necessary Existent as that which is independent of others for its existence and that which deserves existence from itself, it is necessarily known that this amount of conception does not necessitate certainty of the existence of this existent. Rather, after obtaining this conception, the mind remains doubtful whether this reality exists or not. So it is established that in this case the essence can be conceived while doubting existence, and this requires that the essence be other than existence. If this meaning does not entail otherness in this case, then it also does not entail otherness in contingent beings. If they say: The difference is that contingent existence can be separated from existence, so doubt about existence is possible. As for the Necessary Existent, it cannot be conceived insofar as it is Necessary Existent except with the conception of existence, because it is impossible to say that while being Necessary Existent, it does not exist. We say: According to what you mentioned, it would be necessary for everyone who conceives that the Necessary Existent is by itself to be certain that the Necessary Existent exists. And since this is false and corrupt in the natural intellect, we know that it is possible to conceive the Necessary Existent while doubting its existence. If the matter is thus, then its existence must be additional to its essence.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth proof that God's existence (wujud) is additional to (za'id 'ala) His essence (mahiyya). The proof begins by recalling the philosophers' (al-hukama') argument for contingent beings (mumkinat): we can conceive the essence while doubting existence, so essence is distinct from existence. The author, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, then applies the same argument to God (Wajib al-Wujud). He argues that even after conceiving God as self-sufficient and deserving existence from Himself, the mind can doubt whether such a being actually exists. This shows that the essence is conceivable without existence, implying distinctness. The opponent objects that for contingent beings, separation from existence is possible, but for God, conceiving Him as Necessary Existent necessarily includes existence, so doubt is impossible. Al-Razi replies that if that were true, anyone who conceives God as Necessary Existent would be certain of His existence, which is obviously false in the natural intellect. Therefore, it is possible to conceive God while doubting His existence, proving that existence is additional to His essence. The argument follows a pattern of analogy (qiyas) and refutation of a counterargument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since God's existence is necessary, it is impossible to conceive His essence without existence, so essence and existence are identical.
Correction: While God's existence is necessary in reality, conceptually we can separate essence from existence in thought. The ability to doubt existence after conceiving the essence shows a conceptual distinction.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise of the ninth proof that existence is additional to God's essence?
A) God's essence is different from existence because we can conceive one without the other.
B) The philosophers have proven that all existence is the same.
C) God's existence is self-evident and does not need proof.
D) The essence of God is unknowable.

Key Takeaway
The ninth proof demonstrates that even for God, essence and existence are conceptually distinct, based on the possibility of doubting existence after conceiving the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

You have just read a proof that even after conceiving God correctly, doubt about His existence remains possible. Do you personally struggle with such doubt? What is the deepest unresolved question in your heart about God's existence, and what steps will you take this week to address it through study or prayer?
?
Razi argues that if mere conception of God forced certainty, everyone who thinks of God would be certain. But you are not forced. How does this freedom to doubt challenge you to seek conviction actively? What is one concrete action you can take today to move from intellectual acknowledgement to lived certainty?
?
Consider: You can conceive of God as the Necessary Existent, yet still doubt His existence. Does your life reflect the certainty that He exists and deserves worship? If there is a gap, what one area of disobedience or neglect are you willing to change starting today?

The Tenth Proof: The Subject 'Allah' Is Not Existence
Razi analyzes the statement 'Allah is wujub al-wujud' to show the subject is not identical to existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the subject 'Allah' in the statement 'Allah is necessary existence' cannot be existence itself, and how this supports the view that existence is an attribute of the divine essence.

Core Idea
The subject 'Allah' must be distinct from existence, otherwise the statement becomes a meaningless tautology.

Al-Razi continues his logical proofs for the nature of divine existence. In this tenth proof, he dissects the simple statement 'Allah is necessary existence' to reveal a critical distinction. Through rigorous analysis of subject-predicate logic, he demonstrates that 'Allah' cannot refer to existence itself, because that would render the statement absurd—like saying 'the wall is a wall'. This leads to the conclusion that existence is an attribute predicated on a distinct essence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parts of the proposition
Read the passage and identify how Razi breaks down 'Allah is necessary existence' into subject, predicate, and the connecting description. Understand the linguistic framework he uses.
2
Follow the dilemma: is the subject existence or not?
Razi presents two possibilities: the subject is existence itself, or it is something else. See why the first option leads to a tautology or contradiction.
Pay attention to his argument that if subject and predicate are the same, the statement is meaningless.
3
Understand the implication of two existences
If the subject existence is different from the predicate existence, then there would be two existences, and since the nature of existence is one, there is no reason for one to be subject and the other predicate.
This reinforces the absurdity of identifying the subject with existence.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Razi concludes that the subject refers to a reality other than existence, and that existence is an attribute of that reality. This is the desired conclusion that existence is not self-subsistent but a quality of the essence.

الحجة العاشرة : لا شك أن قولنا : الله واجب الوجود ، كلام حق وصدق . فقولنا : الله ،موضوع ، وقولنا : موجود محمول ، وقولنا : واجب صفة لانتساب الموضوع إلى المحمول، ونقول : المراد بالموضوع وهو قولنا الله ، إما أن يكون هو الوجود، أو أمرا مغايرا للوجود ، والأول باطل لوجوه : أحدها : أن على هذا التقدير يصير معنى الكلام أن الوجود واجب الوجود. ولكن هذا باطل ، لأن الوجود الذي جعل موضوعا، إما أن يكون هو الوجود الذي جعل محمولا أو غيره ، والأول باطل لأن جعل الشيء موضوعا لنفسه ومحمولا لنفسه ، كلام فاسد ، عار عن الفائدة. فإن من قال : الجدار : جدار . والمثلث : مثلث . كلامه كلام فاسد ، وإن كان الثاني لزم أن يكون الوجود الذي جعل موضوعا ، مغايرا للوجود الذي جعل محمولا ، فحينئذ يلزم من كونه موجودا : خبرين وأيضا : فلما كانت طبيعة الوجود واحدة ، كان الوجود الذي هو الموضوع مساويا في تمام الماهية للوجود الذي هو المحمول ، وحينئذ لم يكن أحدهما بأن يكون موصوفا والآخر بأن يكون صفة ، أولى من العكس . فيثبت بهذا البرهان : أنا إذا قلنا : الله يجب أن يكون موجودا ، يكن المشار إليه بقولنا نفس الوجودية ، بل حقيقة أخرى سوى الوجودية ، وقد صدق حكم العقل عليها بوجوب اتصافها بالموجودية ، وذلك يدل على أن وجود الله تعالى ليس أمرا قائما بنفسه بل هو صفة لماهية أخرى . وذلك هو المطلوب
The tenth proof: There is no doubt that our statement 'Allah is Wajib al-Wujud (Necessary Existent)' is true and correct. So our statement 'Allah' is the subject, our statement 'existent' is the predicate, and our statement 'necessary' is a description connecting the subject to the predicate. We say: The intended meaning of the subject, which is our statement 'Allah', is either existence or something other than existence. The first is invalid for several reasons. One: On this assumption, the meaning of the statement becomes 'existence is necessary existence'. But this is false, because the existence that is made a subject is either the existence that is made a predicate or something else. The first is false because making a thing a subject of itself and a predicate of itself is corrupt speech devoid of benefit. For if someone says 'the wall is a wall' and 'the triangle is a triangle', his speech is corrupt. If the second [that the subject existence is other than the predicate existence], then it follows that the existence made a subject is other than the existence made a predicate, and then it follows from it being existent that there are two [existences]. Also, since the nature of existence is one, the existence that is the subject equals the existence that is the predicate in complete essence, and then neither is more deserving of being described and the other being a description. Thus, by this proof it is established that when we say 'Allah must be existent', the thing referred to by our statement is not existence itself, but another reality besides existence, and the intellect has truly judged that it must be described by existence. This indicates that the existence of Allah is not something self-subsistent but is an attribute of another essence. That is the required conclusion.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proof that God's existence is an attribute (sifa) of His essence, not identical to it. Al-Razi begins by analyzing the linguistic structure of the proposition 'Allah is Wajib al-Wujud' (Necessary Existent). He identifies 'Allah' as the subject (mawdu'), 'existent' as the predicate (mahmul), and 'necessary' as a description linking subject to predicate. He then argues that the subject 'Allah' cannot refer to existence itself, because that would make the statement tautological: 'existence is necessary existence'. Such a tautology is meaningless, similar to saying 'the wall is a wall'. Furthermore, if subject and predicate are two different existences, then there would be two existences, and since the nature of existence is one, there is no reason for one to be the subject and the other the predicate. The only remaining possibility is that 'Allah' refers to a reality other than existence, and existence is a predicate attributed to that reality. Thus, existence is an attribute (sifa) of an essence other than itself. This proof is based on logical and linguistic reasoning about subject-predicate relations.

Correction: The proof shows that if 'Allah' meant existence, the statement would be a tautology like 'the wall is a wall', which is meaningless. Thus the subject must be a distinct reality.

Checkpoint

C) Because we cannot define Allah.

Key Takeaway
The subject 'Allah' must refer to a reality other than existence, because identifying it with existence makes the statement a meaningless tautology.

?
Razi warns against empty tautologies—statements that say nothing new. In your daily worship, are there phrases you repeat mechanically without their meaning piercing your heart? What would it look like to speak the name 'Allah' as a subject that is utterly real and distinct, not just a placeholder for your thoughts? Take one prayer today and consciously differentiate between the subject (Allah) and the attributes you affirm of Him.

Reflect on a time you tried to 'capture' Allah in your mind or theology. Surrender that attempt now, and let the proof’s conclusion humble you: you can know of Him, but you cannot encompass Him.

Refuting the Unknown Reality Objection
Razi refutes the objection that 'Allah' may be an unknown reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses logical alternatives to refute the objection that we cannot reason about God's existence if His reality is unknown.

Core Idea
Even if Allah's reality is unknown, logic forces an inevitable conclusion.

Al-Razi's tenth proof concluded that existence is an attribute of God. An opponent objects: perhaps 'Allah' refers to an unknown reality whose existence we only know in general. How can we argue about something we do not know? In this passage, Razi shows that even without knowing the specific essence, we can still reason using logical alternatives. The objection fails because it does not escape the force of rational analysis.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection: the opponent claims that the subject 'Allah' might be a reality whose essence we do not know, yet we know it exists in general. This challenges the proof because it questions whether we can attribute anything to that reality.
Note that the objection is about epistemic limitation, not about existence itself.
2
Identify al-Razi's response strategy
Razi does not demand knowledge of the unknown reality. Instead, he points out that regardless of ignorance, the reality must be either existence itself or something else. Both possibilities lead to the conclusion he wants.
This is a purely logical move—no new information about the essence is needed.
3
Work through the two alternatives
First alternative: if the reality is identical with existence, then the same impossibilities (muhalat) from the main proof follow. Second alternative: if it is other than existence, then existence is an attribute of that reality. Both yield the desired result.
The argument is exhaustive: there is no third possibility.
4
Grasp the independence from specificity
Razi concludes that the argument holds whether or not we know the particular nature (hususiyya) of that reality. This shows that theology can proceed using pure logic even when the object is beyond full comprehension.
The passage is cut off, but the key point is established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد من قولنا : الله ، حقيقة لا ندري ماهيتها ولا تعينها ولكنا نعرف في الجملة كونها موجودة ؟ فنقول : هب أنا لا نعرف أن تلك الحقيقة ما هي ؟ إلا أنا نعرف منها أنها إما أن تكون هي الوجود ، أو حقيقة مغايرة للوجود فإن كانت هي الوجود لزمت المحالات وإن كانت حقيقة مغايرة للوجود لزم كون وجود الله تعالى صفة لتلك الحقيقة . وذلك هو المطلوب. ولما كان هذا الكلام الذي يفيد مقصودنا متقرر ، سواء عقلنا خصوصية تلك الحقيقة ، أو لم تعقلها ، لم يكن عدم علمنا بتلك
If they say: Why is it not permissible that the intended meaning of our statement 'Allah' is a reality whose essence and particularity we do not know, but we know in general that it exists? We say: Suppose we do not know what that reality is, but we know of it that it is either existence or a reality other than existence. If it is existence, impossibilities follow. If it is a reality other than existence, then it follows that the existence of Allah is an attribute of that reality. That is the required conclusion. And since this speech which conveys our purpose is established, whether we understand the particularity of that reality or do not understand it, our lack of knowledge of that [particularity] does not [harm the argument].
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the tenth proof and al-Razi's answer. The opponent argues that the subject 'Allah' might refer to an unknown reality (haqiqa) whose specific essence we do not know, but we know in general that it exists. Al-Razi replies by pointing out that even without knowing the specific nature of that reality, we can still reason about it: it must be either existence itself or something other than existence. If it is existence, the same impossibilities (muhalat) from the main proof follow. If it is something else, then existence is an attribute of that reality, which is exactly the conclusion desired. The argument is thus independent of knowing the particular essence (hususiyya) of Allah. The passage ends abruptly, but the point is clear: the objection fails because the logical alternatives leave no escape. This demonstrates the power of purely logical analysis in theology.

The Eleventh Proof: God's Essence Is Not Pure Existence
A logical proof using disjunction to refute that God's essence is pure existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that demonstrates why God's essence cannot be identical to the concept of existence shared by all created things.

Core Idea
God's unique essence cannot be the same as the general concept of existence.

This lesson presents a sophisticated logical proof (burhan) against the claim that God's reality is mere existence without a quiddity. The argument uses a disjunction to show that every possible way of identifying God's essence with existence leads to contradiction or absurdity. By following the proof step by step, you will grasp why classical Islamic theology affirms that God has a unique essence that is not reducible to a common concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Try to follow the logical flow without worrying about every detail yet.
The passage uses technical terms like 'shared concept' (مفهوم مشترك) and 'additional determination' (قيد زائد).
2
Identify the two premises
The author starts with two indisputable facts: (1) God is a specific essence distinct from others, and (2) existence is a concept shared by all existents. Notice how these premises set up the argument.
3
Trace the disjunction (division)
The argument divides into possibilities: either God's essence is identical to the shared existence concept, or it is that concept plus an additional determination. Follow how each branch is examined and rejected.
Pay attention to the logical structure; the proof is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Understand the refutation of the negative determination
The author argues that the additional determination cannot be negative because a negation cannot be part of a positive quiddity. This is a key point in the argument.
5
Analyze the two options for positive determination
If the determination is positive, it can either be the subject with existence as predicate, or vice versa. The author shows both lead to problems, especially the second option which is refuted with two logical principles.
The first option (determination subject, existence predicate) would make existence an accident of a quiddity, which defeats the claim that God is pure existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الحادية عشر في إثبات هذا المطلوب : أن نقول : لا شك أن الحق تعالى ذات متعينة ممتازة بتعينها عن كل ما سواء من الذوات المعينة ، ولا شك أن الوجود من حيث إنه وجود حقيقة يشترك فيها الواجب والممكن والجوهر والعرض ، فنقول : ذاته المخصوصة إما أن يكون هو هذا المفهوم المشترك فيه بين كل الموجودات ، وإما أن يكون هو هذا المفهوم ، مع قيد زائد يقتضي تخصصه وتعينه . والأول باطل ، لأن هذا المفهوم المشترك يصدق عليه كونه مشتركا بين الكل ، وذاته المخصوصة لا يصدق عليها هذا المعنى . فبطل القول بأن ذاته المخصوصة هو هذا المفهوم المشترك بقي أن يقال : إنه هو هذا المفهوم مع قيد زائد . فنقول : ذلك القيد الزائد إما أن يكون سلبيا أو ثبوتيا ، والأول باطل ، لأنه سبحانه من حيث إنه هو ذلك المعين موجود ، وما لأجله التعين جزءا من المعين ، فلو كان ما لأجله حصل التعين قيدا سلبيا ، لزم أن يكون القيد السلبي جزءا من الماهية الثابتة ، وهو محال . وأما إن كان القيد الذي لأجله حصل التعين أمرا ثابتا فإما أن يكون ذلك الأمر المخصوص موضوعا والوجود المشترك فيه محمولا ، وإما أن يكون الأمر بالعكس منه . والأول هو المقصود . لأن على هذا التقدير تكون تلك الهوية المخصوصة المعينة موصوفة بالوجود، فيكون ذلك الوجود عارضا لماهيته ، والثاني باطل لوجهين : أحدهما : أنه ثبت في المنطق أن الأخص بالموضوعية والأعم أولى بالمحمولية . والثاني : وهو أنا لو جعلنا الوجود المطلق موضوعا ، وجعلنا تلك الخصوصية محمولة له ، لزم أن يقال : الوجود المشترك فيه هو تلك الخصوصية ، وحمل تلك الخصوصية عليه ، يمنع من كونه مشتركا فيه بينه وبين غيره ، فلزم أن يقال : الأمر المشترك فيه غير مشترك فيه ، وذلك محال . فثبت : أن القول بأن حقيقته وجود مجرد ، يفضي إلى أقسام باطلة فيكون القول به باطلا
The eleventh proof for establishing this claim: We say: There is no doubt that the Truth Exalted is a specific essence, distinguished by its specificity from every other specific essence. There is no doubt that existence insofar as it is existence is a reality in which the Necessary and the contingent, the substance and the accident, share. We say: His specific essence is either this shared concept among all existents, or it is this concept with an additional determination that necessitates its specification and individuation. The first is false, because this shared concept is predicated as being common to all, but His specific essence is not predicated with this meaning. Thus the claim that His specific essence is this shared concept is false. It remains to be said: It is this concept with an additional determination. We say: That additional determination is either negative or positive. The first is false, because He, glorified, insofar as He is that specific existent, and that by which the specification occurs is part of the specified. If that by which specification occurs were a negative determination, then the negative determination would be part of the established quiddity, which is impossible. As for the determination by which specification occurs being a positive thing, then either that specific thing is the subject and the shared existence is the predicate, or the opposite. The first is the intended one, because on this supposition that specific determined identity is qualified by existence, so that existence is an accident of its quiddity. The second is false for two reasons: One: It is established in logic that the more specific is more fit for subjecthood and the more general for predicatehood. The second: If we make absolute existence the subject and make that specificity its predicate, it would follow that shared existence is that specificity, and predicating that specificity of it prevents it from being shared between it and others, so it would follow that the shared thing is not shared, which is impossible. Thus it is established: The claim that His reality is bare existence leads to absurd divisions, so the claim is false.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage is a logical proof (burhan) against the view that God's essence is pure existence without any additional quiddity. The author begins by stating two premises: first, God is a specific essence distinct from all others; second, existence as such is a common concept shared by all existents (necessary and contingent, substance and accident). Starting from these premises, the argument proceeds by disjunction (division). The author considers two possibilities: either God's specific essence is identical to this shared concept of existence, or it is that concept plus an additional determination. The first is refuted because the shared concept is predicable of many, while God's specific essence is not predicable of many—contradiction. The second possibility is then examined. The additional determination must be either negative or positive. A negative determination is impossible because the determination would be part of the specified quiddity, but a negation cannot be a positive constituent of a quiddity. So the determination must be positive. Then the author examines two ways the positive determination and existence can relate: either the specific determination is the subject and existence the predicate (making existence an accident of a quiddity), or existence is the subject and the determination the predicate. The first is the intended position of the opponent (since they deny a quiddity), but the author will show it leads to absurdity? Actually, the author says "الأول هو المقصود" meaning that the opponent intends the first arrangement? Wait: The author says: "والأول هو المقصود . لأن على هذا التقدير تكون تلك الهوية المخصوصة المعينة موصوفة بالوجود، فيكون ذلك الوجود عارضا لماهيته" — this describes the view that the specific identity is the subject and existence is the predicate, which makes existence an accident of a quiddity. But the opponent (who says God is existence) would not accept a quiddity separate from existence. Actually, the author is refuting the opponent's claim, so the argument is: if the determination is positive, then either existence is predicate or subject. If existence is predicate, then the specific thing is a subject with a quiddity, and existence is accidental—this contradicts the opponent's claim that God is existence itself, not a quiddity with an accident. The opponent might choose the second: existence is subject, specificity is predicate. The author refutes that with two logical reasons: 1) In logic, the more specific is suited to be subject, the more general to be predicate (so existence, being more general, should be predicate, not subject). 2) If existence is subject and specificity predicate, then existence would be that specificity, but predicating specificity of existence would make existence not shared, contradicting its shared nature. Thus all possibilities lead to absurdity, so the claim that God's reality is bare existence is false. The author thus concludes that the view is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that God exists.
Correction: The argument only denies that God's essence is identical to the shared concept of existence; it affirms God's existence as a specific essence.

Checkpoint
According to the eleventh proof, why can't God's specific essence be the shared concept of existence?
A) Because the shared concept is predicated of many, while God's essence is not.
B) Because existence is an accident.
C) Because the shared concept is negative.
D) Because God's essence is beyond existence.

Key Takeaway
God's essence is not pure existence; it is a unique quiddity that existence describes, not the same as the general concept of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This argument shows that even our most refined thoughts about God can be inadequate. How willing are you to let go of comfortable, simplistic ideas about God and embrace the awe-inspiring mystery of His specific essence? What concrete change in your worship or reflection does this require today?

The Twelfth Proof: Why God Must Have a Unique Quiddity
The author presents two arguments proving that God has a distinct quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the unity of existence as a generic nature requires that the Necessary Existent has a distinct quiddity different from all contingent beings.

Core Idea
Existence is one generic nature, so differences force distinct quiddities.

This lesson presents a powerful proof that God is not merely existence-in-general but has a specific reality. The argument begins with a universal premise: all individuals of the same species share the same necessary properties. Since existence is a single generic nature in both the Necessary and the contingent, if God were just existence, He would share all properties with contingents—which is false. Therefore, God must have a unique quiddity. The lesson also provides an additional argument from the multiplicity of existents.

Guided Path
1
Understand the universal premise
Recognize that all rational people agree: individuals of a single species must be equal in their necessary concomitants (lawazim). This is the foundation of the proof.
This premise is considered self-evident by reason.
2
Apply the premise to existence
The author asserts that existence, whether in God or in creation, is a single generic nature. Therefore, whatever applies to one should apply to the other.
3
Observe the false conclusion
If God and contingents had the same necessary properties, then God would be contingent, which is absurd. Therefore, their realities must differ.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Consider the additional argument
Existence only becomes multiple due to different receptacles (quiddities). Since existence is multiplied, there must be different quiddities, including a distinct quiddity for God.
This reinforces the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية عشر : أجمع العقلاء على أن أفراد النوع الواحد يجب تساويها في اللوازم ، وصريح العقل شاهد بصحة هذه المقدمة . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : الوجود في حق واجب الوجود ، والوجود في حق ممكن الوجود : طبيعة نوعية واحدة. فوجب أن يصح على كل واحد منهما ، ما يصح على الآخر . ولما كان ذلك باطلا ، علمنا أن الحقيقة المخصوصة لواجب الوجود لذاته حقيقة ، تخالف سائر الحقائق. وأيضا : فقد اتفقت الحكماء على أن الطبيعة النوعية إنما بعرض لها التعدد بسبب القوابل والماهية الموصوفة بالوجدان ، جارية مجرى القوابل بتلك الوجودات . فثبت أنه لولا الماهيات المختلفة لامتنع وقوع التعدد في طبيعة الوجود ، وحيث وقع التعدد فيه، علمنا أن وقوع التعدد فيها ، إنما كان بسبب الماهيات المختلفة. فهذه البراهين التي ذكرناها ولخصناها : وجوه ظاهرة جلية قطعية في إفساد هذا المذهب .
The twelfth proof: All rational people agree that the individuals of a single species must be equal in their necessary concomitants, and clear reason testifies to the correctness of this premise. When this is established, we say: Existence in the case of the Necessary Existent and existence in the case of the contingent existent is a single generic nature. Therefore, whatever applies to one must apply to the other. Since that is false, we know that the specific reality of the Necessary Existent in itself is a reality that differs from all other realities. Also, the philosophers agree that the generic nature only becomes multiple due to the receptacles and the quiddity qualified by existence, functioning as receptacles for those existences. Thus it is established that were it not for the different quiddities, multiplicity in the nature of existence would be impossible, and since multiplicity occurs in it, we know that its multiplicity is only due to the different quiddities. These proofs that we have mentioned and summarized are clear, evident, and decisive arguments for refuting this doctrine.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a proof by consensus (ijma' of rational people) and by philosophical principle. The author first states a premise universally accepted: all individuals of one species share the same necessary accidents (lawazim). If a property applies to one, it applies to all. Then he asserts that existence, whether in the Necessary Existent or in contingent beings, is a single generic nature. Therefore, whatever is true of one would be true of the other, but we know that the Necessary Existent has attributes not shared by contingents (e.g., necessity, being uncaused). Thus the premise forces us to conclude that the reality of the Necessary Existent is different in kind from other realities—it is not just existence but a specific quiddity. The author then adds an additional argument: philosophers agree that a generic nature only becomes multiple through different receptacles (quiddities). Since existence is multiplied among different existents, there must be different quiddities to receive it. Hence, the Necessary Existent must have a distinct quiddity. Finally, the author states that these proofs (including the eleventh) are clear, evident, and decisive in refuting the doctrine that God is mere existence.

Key Terms
النوع الواحد
اللوازم
طبيعة نوعية
الحقيقة المخصوصة
القوابل
الماهيات المختلفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof denies that God has existence; it is often misunderstood as arguing that God does not exist at all.
Correction: The proof actually shows that God's existence is not a generic nature but a specific reality distinct from others. God exists, but His quiddity is unique.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude from the fact that existence is a single generic nature?
A) That the Necessary Existent and contingent existent share all attributes.
B) That the Necessary Existent must have a specific reality different from other realities.
C) That existence is an accident.
D) That the Necessary Existent does not exist.

Key Takeaway
God's existence is not a generic nature; He has a unique quiddity that distinguishes Him from all other beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever reduce your concept of God to a generic 'higher power' or 'force' that is just like other beings but bigger? How does this proof challenge you to see Him as utterly unique, with a quiddity that no other being shares? What would change in your worship if you truly lived with this reality?
?
The proof shows that God's reality is distinct from all else. Look at your life: where do you treat God as if He were just one among many priorities, instead of the One whose quiddity is incomparably different? Will you commit today to letting this truth reshape how you orient your heart and actions?

Ibn Sina's Proof: God's Existence Is Self-Subsistent
Ibn Sina's disjunctive argument for self-subsistence of God's existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's disjunctive argument that God's existence cannot be an accident of any quiddity, because it must be either self-subsistent or contingent, and contingency is incompatible with God's necessity.

Core Idea
God's existence is either self-subsistent or contingent; contingency is impossible, so it is self-subsistent.

Ibn Sina, known as the Chief Master, presents a concise but powerful proof that God's existence cannot be a mere accident inhering in some quiddity. In this passage from his Ishārāt, he sets up a logical disjunction that forces the conclusion that God's existence is necessarily self-subsistent. The author of our text presents this argument and promises to strengthen it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall flow of the argument without analyzing details.
Note the use of the conditional 'either...or' (إما...وإما).
2
Identify the two alternatives
Spot the two possibilities: God's existence is either independent and self-subsistent, or it depends on something else.
These are the only logical options.
3
Trace the consequence of the second alternative
If God's existence depends on another, it becomes contingent (mumkin li-dhatihi). Since we know God is necessary, this branch is false, leaving the first as true.
Contingency contradicts God's necessity, so the first must hold.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج الشيخ الرئيس : [أبو علي] في بيان أن وجود الله تعالى يمتنع كونه عارضا [لشيء] من الماهيات بوجه واحد ذكره في كتاب الإشارات . وأنا أذكر ذلك الوجه ، وأضم إليه تقريرات قوية ، وبيانات كاملة . فنقول : وجود الله تعالى إما أن يكون مستقلا بنفسه قائما بذاته ، من غير أن يكون تحققه محتاجا إلى تحقق غيره، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك ، بل يكون تحققه محتاجا إلى تحقق غيره. فإن كان الحق هو الأول ، كان ذلك الوجود قائما بذاته مستقلا بنفسه ، فلا يكون عارضا لشيء من الماهيات . وإن كان الحق هو الثاني ، وهو كون ذلك الوجود غير مستقل بنفسه بل يكون مفتقرا إلى غيره ، فنقول : فذلك الوجود مفتقر إلى غيره ، وكل مفتقر إلى غيره فهو ممكن لذاته ، فذلك الوجود ممكن لذاته .
And the Chief Master [Abu Ali] argued, in explaining that it is impossible for God's existence to be an accident of any quiddity, with one argument he mentioned in the Book of Pointers (al-Isharat). I will mention that argument and add to it strong reinforcements and complete clarifications. We say: God's existence is either independent in itself, subsistent by its essence, without its actuality needing the actuality of another, or it is not like that, rather its actuality needs the actuality of another. If the truth is the first, then that existence is subsistent by itself, independent in itself, so it is not an accident of any quiddity. If the truth is the second, that this existence is not independent in itself but depends on another, we say: That existence depends on another, and everything that depends on another is contingent in itself, so that existence is contingent in itself.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of Ibn Sina (the 'Chief Master') from his Kitab al-Isharat. The author will present Ibn Sina's argument and then add his own reinforcements (though the text here only gives the base argument). The proof is a disjunction: either God's existence is self-subsistent (independent of anything else) or it is not. If it is self-subsistent, then it cannot be an accident of any quiddity because an accident inheres in a subject and depends on it. If it is not self-subsistent, meaning it depends on something else for its actuality, then it is contingent (mumkin li-dhatihi), because dependence on another is the mark of contingency. But we know God is necessary (wajib al-wujud) by Himself, so this second branch is false. Therefore, the first branch is true, and God's existence is self-subsistent and not an accident of a quiddity. The author promises to strengthen this argument, but the base is clear: it rules out the possibility that God's existence is an accident inhering in some quiddity—whether that quiddity be his essence (as the opponent's view would imply) or another.

Key Terms
الشيخ الرئيس
مستقل بنفسه
قائم بذاته
عارض
ماهية
مفتقر إلى غيره
ممكن لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Ibn Sina is denying God's essence or that God's existence could be an accident of His essence.
Correction: Ibn Sina is proving that God's existence cannot be an accident of any quiddity—neither His own essence nor another's—because it is necessarily self-subsistent.

Checkpoint
What is the second alternative in Ibn Sina's disjunction about God's existence?
A) That God's existence is independent and self-subsistent.
B) That God's existence is dependent on another.
C) That God's existence is an accident of a quiddity.
D) That God's existence is not real.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's disjunction shows that God's existence must be self-subsistent, because the alternative leads to contingency, which contradicts divine necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's existence were dependent on anything, how would that affect your trust in His ability to sustain you? Does your heart sometimes act as if God's existence is an afterthought rather than the foundation of all reality?
?
Ibn Sina argued that God's existence cannot be an accident. Is God's existence an 'accident' in your daily life—something you add on when convenient? What would change if you allowed His self-subsistent existence to be the very ground of your morning?
?
This proof shows that God alone is self-subsistent, while everything else depends on Him. Are you living in that dependence, or are you trying to stand independently from the One who needs nothing? What is one area where you must stop pretending to be self-sufficient?

The Twelfth Proof: Unity of Nature Implies Equality of Concomitants
Sanusi argues that existence cannot be a generic nature shared by God and creatures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle that individuals of the same species are equal in their necessary concomitants is used to prove that God's essence is unique and not a mere attribute of existence.

Core Idea
God's reality differs from all others because His existence is not a generic attribute.

The twelfth proof builds on a universal rational principle: things of the same kind share the same necessary properties. Sanusi applies this to existence itself, showing that God's existence cannot be the same as that of creatures. The argument is both philosophical and decisive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key premise
Read the opening statement carefully: 'The rational people have agreed that individuals of the same species must be equal in their necessary concomitants.' This is the foundational principle. Note that Sanusi says 'clear intellect testifies' to it, meaning it is self-evident.
2
Follow the application to existence
Sanusi then treats 'existence' as a single specific nature predicated of both God and creatures. If so, the same concomitants would apply to both. But that leads to a contradiction (since God is necessary, creatures are possible). Therefore, the specific reality of God must be different.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Understand the second argument from quiddities
Sanusi adds a supporting argument from the philosophers: a generic nature becomes multiple only due to different receptacles (quiddities). Since multiplicity in existence is observed, there must be different quiddities. This further proves that God's quiddity (essence) is unique.
This reinforces the conclusion without relying on the first premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية عشر: أجمع العقلاء على أن أفراد النوع الواحد يجب تساويها في اللوازم، وصريح العقل شاهد بصحة هذه المقدمة. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: الوجود في حق واجب الوجود، والوجود في حق ممكن الوجود: طبيعة نوعية واحدة. فوجب أن يصح على كل واحد منهما، ما يصح على الآخر. ولما كان ذلك باطلاً، علمنا أن الحقيقة المخصوصة لواجب الوجود لذاته حقيقة، تخالف سائر الحقائق. وأيضاً: فقد اتفقت الحكماء على أن الطبيعة النوعية إنما يعرض لها التعدد بسبب القوابل والماهية الموصوفة بالوجود، جارية مجرى القوابل بتلك الوجودات. فثبت أنه لولا الماهيات المختلفة لامتنع وقوع التعدد في طبيعة الوجود، وحيث وقع التعدد فيها، علمنا أن وقوع التعدد فيها إنما كان بسبب الماهيات المختلفة. فهذه البراهين التي ذكرناها ولخصناها: وجوه ظاهرة جلية قطعية في إفساد هذا المذهب.
The twelfth proof: The rational people have agreed that individuals of the same species must be equal in their necessary concomitants, and clear intellect testifies to the soundness of this premise. And when this is established, we say: Existence (al-wujud) with respect to the Necessary Existent (wajib al-wujud) and existence with respect to the possible existent (mumkin al-wujud) is a single specific nature. So it must be that whatever is valid for one of them is valid for the other. And when that is false, we know that the specific reality (al-haqiqa al-makhsusa) of the Necessary Existent in His essence is a reality that is different from all other realities. Also: The philosophers have agreed that specific nature only becomes multiple due to the receptacles (al-qawabil) and the quiddity (al-mahiyya) described by existence, running in the course of receptacles for those existences. So it is established that were it not for the different quiddities (al-mahiyyat), multiplicity in the nature of existence would be impossible; and since multiplicity occurs in it, we know that the occurrence of multiplicity in it is only due to the different quiddities. So these proofs that we have mentioned and summarized are clear, evident, and decisive proofs for the corruption of this doctrine.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical proof against the doctrine that God's existence is an accident of His essence. The author begins by stating a universally accepted premise: individuals belonging to the same species must share the same necessary concomitants (lawazim). Reason itself attests to this. Then he applies it: 'existence' when predicated of the Necessary Existent (God) and of possible beings is supposedly a single generic nature. If so, whatever is true of one (e.g., being necessary or being possible) would have to be true of the other. But that is false (since God is necessary, creatures are possible). Therefore, the specific reality of God must be entirely different from all other realities. The author adds a second argument from the philosophers: a generic nature only becomes multiple (i.e., instantiated in many individuals) due to the receptacles (qawabil) or quiddities that receive it. Without differentiate quiddities, multiplicity in existence would be impossible. Since we observe multiplicity, it must be due to different quiddities. This reinforces that God's reality is not a mere attribute of existence but a unique essence. The passage concludes that these proofs are decisive for refuting the false doctrine.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
ممكن الوجود
طبيعة نوعية
ماهية
لوازم
حقيقة مخصوصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence is a univocal concept applying equally to God and creatures, making God's essence an instance of a generic attribute.
Correction: The proof shows that if existence were a generic nature, then God and creatures would share the same concomitants, which is false. Therefore, the specific reality of God is fundamentally different.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise of the twelfth proof?
A) All individuals of the same species are equal in their accidental attributes.
B) All individuals of the same species must be equal in their necessary concomitants.
C) Existence is a genus with many species.
D) The Necessary Existent is the cause of all possible beings.

Key Takeaway
The twelfth proof shows that God's essence is uniquely different from all creatures because existence cannot be a generic attribute shared equally.

?
The proof shows that you cannot ascribe to God what belongs to creatures. Are you willing to surrender the comfortable, manageable god you’ve constructed in your mind—the one you think you can predict or negotiate with—and submit to the utterly unique Reality whose essence is beyond comparison? What concrete step will you take this week to worship Him as He truly is, not as you imagine Him?
?
Because God’s existence is not a generic attribute, your own existence is a sheer gift from Him, moment by moment. Does your daily life reflect that dependence? When was the last time you paused, not just to thank God for blessings, but to acknowledge that your very being is sustained by His unique, non-generic existence? Will you do that right now, before moving on?

The Core Disjunction: Self-Subsistent or Dependent
Sanusi introduces Ibn Sina's proof with a disjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial disjunction in Ibn Sina's proof: God's existence is either self-subsistent and independent, or it is dependent on something else.

Core Idea
God's existence is either self-sufficient or requires a cause—no middle ground.

Ibn Sina begins his proof with a clear either/or: God's existence must be either independent or not. This binary sets the stage for the entire argument. If it is independent, then it cannot be an accident of any essence. If it is dependent, then we must trace its cause. Let's examine this first branch.

Guided Path
1
Read the disjunctive premise
Read the first part of the passage where Sanusi presents Ibn Sina's argument. Notice the clear 'إما... وإما' (either...or) structure.
This is a classic logical disjunction.
2
Understand the first option
The first possibility is that God's existence is 'مستقل بنفسه' (independent in itself). Identify why this would mean it cannot be an accident.
Accidents depend on a subject; a self-subsistent existence cannot be accidental.
3
Pause before the second option
The second option (dependent) is introduced but not yet analyzed. Recognize that the rest of the proof will handle this branch.
The argument splits here; the next lessons cover the dependent branch.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج الشيخ الرئيس أبو علي في بيان أن وجود الله تعالى يمتنع كونه عارضا لشيء من الماهيات بوجه واحد ذكره في كتاب الإشارات. وأنا أذكر ذلك الوجه، وأضم إليه تقريرات قوية، وبيانات كاملة. فنقول: وجود الله تعالى إما أن يكون مستقلا بنفسه قائما بذاته، من غير أن يكون تحققه محتاجا إلى تحقق غيره، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك، بل يكون تحققه محتاجا إلى تحقق غيره. فإن كان الحق هو الأول، كان ذلك الوجود قائما بذاته مستقلا بنفسه، فلا يكون عارضا لشيء من الماهيات. وإن كان الحق هو الثاني، وهو كون ذلك الوجود غير مستقل بنفسه بل يكون مفتقرا إلى غيره، فنقول: فذلك الوجود مفتقر إلى غيره، وكل مفتقر إلى غيره فهو ممكن لذاته، فذلك الوجود ممكن لذاته. وكل ممكن لذاته فلا بد له من سبب، وذلك السبب إما تلك الماهية أو غيرها. ولا جائز أن يكون سبب ذلك الوجود هو تلك الماهية، لأن كون تلك الماهية مقتضية لذلك الوجود، إما أن يكون مشروطا بكونها موجودة أو لا يكون مشروطا بهذا الشرط. ولا جائز أن يكون اقتضاء تلك الماهية لذلك الوجود، مشروطا بكونها موجودة، لأن الوجود الذي هو شرط الاقتضاء، إن كان هو الوجود الذي هو الأثر لزم كون الشيء الواحد شرطا لنفسه، وهو محال، وإن كان غيره لزم أن يكون الشيء الواحد موجودا مرتين، وهو محال. ولأن الكلام في الوجود الثاني كالكلام في الأول، يلزم إما التسلسل، وإما الدور وهما محالان. هذا كله إذا قلنا: إن اقتضاء تلك الماهية لذلك الوجود، مشروط بكون تلك الماهية موجودة. أما إن قلنا: إن هذا الشرط غير معتبر، فحينئذ يكون هذا قولا بأن ما ليس بموجود، يكون مقتضيا للوجود، وذلك محال في بديهة العقل، لأن الشيء ما لم يكن موجودا في نفسه، يمتنع كونه علة في وجود غيره. وهذا كله إذا قلنا: العلة المقتضية لذلك الوجود هي تلك الماهية. أما إذا قلنا: العلة المقتضية لذلك الوجود شيء آخر سوى تلك الماهية، فحينئذ يكون وجود واجب الوجود لذاته، مستفادا من سبب منفصل، فيكون واجب الوجود لذاته واجب الوجود بغيره، وذلك محال. فثبت أن القول بأن وجود واجب الوجود لذاته عارض لماهيته: يفضي إلى هذه المحالات، فوجب أن يكون القول به محالا. فثبت أنه لا حقيقة له إلا الوجود المجرد عن جميع الماهيات. وهذا هو تقرير هذه الحجة على أقوى الوجوه.
And the Chief Master Abu Ali argued in explaining that the existence of God, exalted, is impossible to be an accident of any quiddity, by a single argument he mentioned in the Book of Pointers. And I will mention that argument and append to it strong expositions and complete explanations. So we say: The existence of God, exalted, is either independent in itself, subsisting by itself, without its reality needing the realization of something else, or it is not like that, but rather its reality needs the realization of something else. If the truth is the first, then that existence is subsisting by itself, independent in itself, so it is not an accident of any quiddity. If the truth is the second—i.e., that existence is not independent in itself but rather is in need of another—then we say: That existence is in need of another, and everything in need of another is possible in itself, so that existence is possible in itself. And everything possible in itself necessarily requires a cause, and that cause is either that quiddity or something else. And it is not possible that the cause of that existence is that quiddity, because the fact that that quiddity necessitates that existence either depends on its being existent or does not depend on this condition. And it is not possible that the necessitation of that quiddity for that existence is conditioned upon its being existent, because if the existence which is the condition of necessitation is the same as the existence which is the effect, then it would entail that one thing is a condition for itself, which is impossible. And if it is different, then it would entail that one thing exists twice, which is impossible. And because the discussion about the second existence is like the discussion about the first, it would entail either infinite regress or circularity, both of which are impossible. All this is if we say: the necessitation of that quiddity for that existence is conditioned upon that quiddity being existent. But if we say: this condition is not considered, then this would be a statement that what is not existent can necessitate existence, and that is impossible in the intuition of the intellect, because a thing, as long as it is not existent in itself, cannot be a cause for the existence of another. And all this is if we say: the necessitating cause of that existence is that quiddity. But if we say: the necessitating cause of that existence is something other than that quiddity, then the existence of the Necessary Existent in itself would be derived from a separate cause, thus the Necessary Existent in itself would be necessary by another, and that is impossible. So it is established that the statement that the existence of the Necessary Existent in itself is an accident of His quiddity leads to these impossibilities, so it must be impossible to hold it. So it is established that He has no reality except existence abstracted from all quiddities. And this is the exposition of this argument in the strongest manner.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a proof by Avicenna (Ibn Sina) against the view that God's existence is an accidental attribute of His essence, presented and strengthened by the author. The argument proceeds by a disjunction: God's existence is either self-subsistent and independent, or not. If it is self-subsistent, then it cannot be an accident (since accidents need a subject). If it is dependent, then it is possible in itself and needs a cause. That cause could be the quiddity itself or something else. If the quiddity is the cause, the author examines two possibilities: either the quiddity's causation is conditioned on its own existence or not. If conditioned, then either the condition is identical with the effect (leading to self-causing, impossible) or different (leading to double existence, also impossible). If not conditioned, then a non-existent thing would be causing existence, which is intuitively absurd. If the cause is something other than the quiddity, then God's existence would be derived from another, making Him necessary by another (not by Himself), which contradicts the definition of the Necessary Existent. Therefore, all options lead to impossibility, proving that God's existence cannot be an accident; rather, His reality is pure existence free from quiddities. The author concludes by affirming this as the strongest formulation of the argument.

Key Terms
الشيخ الرئيس
وجود الله
عارض
ماهية
مستقل بنفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the disjunction presented, if God's existence is independent, what does it mean?
A) It can be an accident of something else.
B) It is self-subsistent and cannot be an accident.
C) It is dependent on a quiddity.
D) It is possible in itself.

Key Takeaway
The first branch of the disjunction shows that if God's existence is self-subsistent, it cannot be an accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God's existence is independent and self-subsistent, needing nothing. But you often act as if your own existence, your job, your relationships, or your health are independent. When was the last moment today you felt anxious because you forgot that only God is truly self-sufficient? Will you pause right now and say, 'I depend on Him alone'?
?
If God's existence cannot be an accident, then it is essential. Your existence is contingent—a gift from Him. Does your daily life reflect that you are a dependent being? Name one thing you treat as if it is essential to your happiness that is actually an accident—something you could lose. How will you reattach your heart to the only Self-Subsistent One?
?
The argument uses a basic logical disjunction. Have you ever honestly faced the disjunction in your own belief: either God is independent or not? If you truly believe He is independent, does your reliance on His creation mirror that belief? Commit today to one concrete action that shows your total dependence on Him.
